The Four Levels of Existence (Maratib al-Wujud)
Explains the four ranks of existence and the chain of signification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four levels of existence and explain the direction of signification among them.

Core Idea
Words are third in existence: they signify mental forms, which are likenesses of external objects.

Before we understand how words can be universal, we must first grasp where words sit in the order of existence. The author introduces a four-rank framework that places external objects first, then mental forms, then words, and finally writing. This hierarchy explains how knowledge and language are connected to reality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four ranks
List the four levels in order: existence in external objects (a'yan), in minds (adhhan), in words (alfaz), and in writing (kitaba). Notice that words are in the third rank.
This order is logical, not chronological.
2
Trace the direction of signification
Writing indicates words, words indicate mental meanings, and mental meanings are likenesses of external objects. So signification flows from outer to inner.
3
Grasp the definition of knowledge
The author defines knowledge ('ilm) as a mental likeness (mithal) that corresponds to the external object as perceived by the senses. Without this likeness, no knowledge occurs.
4
Note the conventionality of words and writing
External and mental existence are universal across lands and nations, but words and writing vary because they are based on convention (wad') and agreement (istilah).
This explains why languages differ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسمة الثالثة : في بيان رتبة الألفاظ من مراتب الوجود: اعلم أن المراتب فيما نقصده أربعة واللفظ في الرتبة الثالثة، فإن للشيء وجوداً في الأعيان ثم في الأذهان، ثم في الألفاظ ثم في الكتابة، فالكتابة دالة على اللفظ واللفظ دال على المعنى الذي في النفس والذي في النفس هو مثال الموجود في الأعيان. فما لم يكن للشيء ثبوت في نفسه لم يرتسم في النفس مثاله ومهما ارتسم في النفس مثاله فهو العلم به إذ لا معنى للعلم إلا مثال يحصل في النفس مطابق لما هو له في الحسّ وهو المعلوم، وما لم يظهر هذا الأثر في النفس لا ينتظم لفظ يدلّ على ذلك الأثر ، وما لم ينتظم اللفظ الذي ترتب فيه الأصوات والحروف لا ترتسم كتابة للدلالة عليه والوجود في الأعيان والأذهان لا يختلف بالبلاد والأمم، بخلاف الألفاظ والكتابة فإنهما دالتان بالوضع والاصطلاح.
وعند هذا نقول : من زعم أن الاسم المفرد لا يقتضي الاستغراق ظنّ أنه موضوع بإزاء الموجود في الأعيان فإنهما أشخاص معينة إذ الدينار الموجود شخص معين، فإن جمعت أشخاص سُميت دنانير، ولم يعرف أن الدينار الشخصي المعين يرتسم منه في النفس أثر هو مثاله وعلم به وتصوّر له، وذلك المثال يطابق ذلك الشخص وسائر أشخاص الدنانير الموجودة والممكن وجودها فتكون الصورة الثابتة في النفس من حيث مطابقتها لكلّ دينار يفرض صورة كلّية لا شخصية؛ فإن اعتقدت أن اسم الدينار دليل على الأثر في النفس لا على المؤثر وذلك الأثر كلّي، كان الاسم كلياً لا محالة ، وما قدمناه من الترتيب يعرفك أن الألفاظ لها دلالات على ما في النفوس، وما في النفوس مثال لما في الأعيان وسيأتي مزيد بيان للمعاني الكلية المرتسمة في النفوس مشاهدة الأشخاص الجزئية في كتاب «أحكام الوجود ولواحقه»
The third division: On explaining the rank of words among the ranks of existence. Know that the ranks concerning what we intend are four, and the word is in the third rank. For a thing has existence in external objects, then in minds, then in words, then in writing. So writing indicates the word, and the word indicates the meaning that is in the soul, and that which is in the soul is the likeness of what exists in external objects. For if something has no stability in itself, its likeness is not impressed in the soul; and whenever its likeness is impressed in the soul, that is knowledge of it, for knowledge is nothing but a likeness that occurs in the soul corresponding to what it is for in sensation, and that is the known object. And if this impression does not appear in the soul, no word is organized to indicate that impression; and if the word in which sounds and letters are arranged is not organized, no writing is depicted to indicate it. And existence in external objects and minds does not vary by lands and nations, unlike words and writing, for they are indicative by convention and agreement.
And at this point we say: Whoever claims that the singular noun does not imply comprehension thought that it is placed in correspondence to what exists in external objects, for they are specific individuals, since the existing dinar is a specific individual; if you gather individuals they are called dinars. And he did not know that from the specific individual dinar an impression is depicted in the soul, which is its likeness and knowledge of it and conception of it; and that likeness corresponds to that individual and to all individuals of dinars existing and possible to exist, so the form established in the soul, by virtue of its correspondence to every dinar that is assumed, is a universal form, not a particular one. If you believe that the name 'dinar' is indicative of the impression in the soul and not of the object that produces the impression, and that impression is universal, then the name is necessarily universal. And what we have presented of the ordering makes you know that words have indications to what is in the souls, and what is in the souls is a likeness to what is in external objects. And further clarification of the universal meanings impressed in the souls due to observing partial individuals will come in the book 'Ahkam al-Wujud wa Lawahiqihi' (Rulings of Existence and its Consequences).
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents the 'third division' (al-qisma al-thalitha) which explains the rank of words among the levels of existence. The author states that there are four levels: (1) existence in external objects (wujud fi al-a'yan), (2) existence in minds (fi al-adhhan), (3) existence in words (fi al-alfaz), and (4) existence in writing (fi al-kitaba). Words occupy the third rank. Writing indicates words, and words indicate mental meanings, which in turn are likenesses of external objects. Knowledge is defined as a mental form (mithal) that corresponds to the object as perceived by the senses.

The author then applies this to refute those who deny that singular nouns with the definite article imply generality. The error arises from assuming that the word refers directly to the external particular (e.g., a specific dinar). In reality, the word refers to the mental form (al-athar fi al-nafs), which is universal because it matches all possible instances of dinars. Therefore, the word is universal. The author emphasizes that words are conventional (wad'iyya) and vary across nations, while external and mental existence are universal.

The passage concludes by promising further detail on how universal meanings arise from observing particular individuals, to be treated in another work. The key lesson is that universality resides in the mental concept, not in the external particular, so words that signify those concepts are inherently universal regardless of how many actual individuals exist.

Key Terms
مراتب الوجود
مثال
وضع
الوجود في الأعيان
الوجود في الأذهان
الوجود في الألفاظ
الوجود في الكتابة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the definition of knowledge ('ilm)?
A) The external object as it is in itself
B) A word that signifies a meaning
C) A mental likeness (mithal) that corresponds to the sensed object
D) A written text that describes the object

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is a mental form that matches the external object, and words and writing are conventional signs for that mental form at the third and fourth levels of existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Map the four levels of existence for the concept of 'salat' (prayer): identify its external reality (the actual prayer performed), its mental form (the concept in the mind), its verbal expression (the word 'salat'), and its written form. Explain what makes the verbal expression conventional vs. the mental form universal.

Why Singular Nouns Are Universal
Argues that singular nouns imply generality because they signify universal mental forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that singular nouns (like 'dinar') indicate universal mental forms, not external particulars.

Core Idea
A word like 'dinar' is universal because it points to a mental form that matches every possible dinar.

Some have claimed that singular nouns with the definite article do not imply generality because they refer to external particulars. The author refutes this by applying the four-level framework. He shows that words denote mental forms, which are universal by nature, so the words themselves become universal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the erroneous view
The author describes those who deny that a singular noun (like 'al-dinar') implies universality (istighraq). Their reasoning: the word is placed for an external particular, so it is a specific individual.
This is a common misunderstanding in logic and usul al-fiqh.
2
Follow the author's correction using the mental impression
The author says that even from a specific dinar, a mental form (athar) is impressed in the soul. This form is a likeness of that particular dinar, but it also corresponds to all possible dinars. Therefore, the mental form is universal.
3
Distinguish between the referent of the word
The word 'dinar' does not refer to the external particular; it refers to the mental impression (al-athar fi al-nafs). Since that impression is universal, the word becomes universal.
4
Conclude the universality of the name
If you believe that the name indicates the mental form, then the name is necessarily universal. The author refers back to the ordering of existence to reinforce this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسمة الثالثة : في بيان رتبة الألفاظ من مراتب الوجود: اعلم أن المراتب فيما نقصده أربعة واللفظ في الرتبة الثالثة، فإن للشيء وجوداً في الأعيان ثم في الأذهان، ثم في الألفاظ ثم في الكتابة، فالكتابة دالة على اللفظ واللفظ دال على المعنى الذي في النفس والذي في النفس هو مثال الموجود في الأعيان. فما لم يكن للشيء ثبوت في نفسه لم يرتسم في النفس مثاله ومهما ارتسم في النفس مثاله فهو العلم به إذ لا معنى للعلم إلا مثال يحصل في النفس مطابق لما هو له في الحسّ وهو المعلوم، وما لم يظهر هذا الأثر في النفس لا ينتظم لفظ يدلّ على ذلك الأثر ، وما لم ينتظم اللفظ الذي ترتب فيه الأصوات والحروف لا ترتسم كتابة للدلالة عليه والوجود في الأعيان والأذهان لا يختلف بالبلاد والأمم، بخلاف الألفاظ والكتابة فإنهما دالتان بالوضع والاصطلاح.
وعند هذا نقول : من زعم أن الاسم المفرد لا يقتضي الاستغراق ظنّ أنه موضوع بإزاء الموجود في الأعيان فإنهما أشخاص معينة إذ الدينار الموجود شخص معين، فإن جمعت أشخاص سُميت دنانير، ولم يعرف أن الدينار الشخصي المعين يرتسم منه في النفس أثر هو مثاله وعلم به وتصوّر له، وذلك المثال يطابق ذلك الشخص وسائر أشخاص الدنانير الموجودة والممكن وجودها فتكون الصورة الثابتة في النفس من حيث مطابقتها لكلّ دينار يفرض صورة كلّية لا شخصية؛ فإن اعتقدت أن اسم الدينار دليل على الأثر في النفس لا على المؤثر وذلك الأثر كلّي، كان الاسم كلياً لا محالة ، وما قدمناه من الترتيب يعرفك أن الألفاظ لها دلالات على ما في النفوس، وما في النفوس مثال لما في الأعيان وسيأتي مزيد بيان للمعاني الكلية المرتسمة في النفوس مشاهدة الأشخاص الجزئية في كتاب «أحكام الوجود ولواحقه»

Understanding Mushakkik: Terms with Varied Intensity
Author defines mushakkik and distinguishes it from mutawati' with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish mushakkik from mutawati' and identify the three subtypes of mushakkik with examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Mushakkik terms share a meaning but vary in intensity or relation—the middle ground between univocal and equivocal.

Not all words apply equally to everything they name. Some, like 'white' for snow and ivory, share a meaning but with different intensity. This nuance is captured by the concept of mushakkik. In Islamic sciences, terms like 'iman' (faith) or 'wajib' (obligatory) often function as mushakkik, and failing to recognize this can lead to theological or legal confusion. This lesson will equip you to identify and classify mushakkik terms.

Guided Path
1
Define mushakkik and contrast with mutawati'
Read the first part of the passage. The author says that mushakkik is 'what admits intensity and weakness' unlike mutawati' which applies equally. Understand that mutawati' terms like 'animal' for Zaid and horse have no variation, while mushakkik terms like 'whiteness' have gradation.
Keep in mind: mushakkik shares a core meaning; variation is in degree or relation, not in distinct meanings (that would be mushtarak).
2
Identify the three subtypes of mushakkik
The passage lists three: absolute (mushakkik fīhā bi-shidda wa-du'f), relative to a single principle (mabda'), relative to a single end (ghāya), and both principle and end. Extract each from the examples: 'medical' (relative to medicine as principle), 'healthy' (relative to health as end), and 'divine' (relative to God as both principle and end).
The absolute subtype refers to intensity itself; the relational subtypes involve causal or teleological relations.
3
Apply the concept to Islamic science examples
Consider the term 'dalīl' (evidence) in usul al-fiqh. It applies to both qat'i (definitive) and zanni (probable) evidence, with a shared meaning but different strength—this is mushakkik. Similarly, 'salah' (prayer) applies to obligatory and supererogatory prayers with different emphasis. Generate your own examples from kalam or fiqh.
Ensure the example shares a core meaning; if meanings are completely different, it's mushtarak, not mushakkik.
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the MCQ. Use the distinction: Option A is mutawati' because 'animal' applies equally; B is mushakkik because whiteness varies; C is mushtarak (eye has two distinct meanings); D could be mutawati' if 'book' meaning is the same (Quran and notebook share the meaning of written compilation). The correct answer is B.
This reinforces the key difference: mushakkik must have a single core meaning with variation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقول بالشدّة والضعف فيتصور فيما يقبل الشدة والضعف كالبياض للعاج والثلج ، فإنه لا يقال عليهما بالتواطؤ المطلق المتساوي، بل أحدهما أشدّ فيه من الآخر . أما الحيوان لزيد ،وعمرو والفرس والثور فلا يتطرّق إليه شيء من هذا التفاوت بحال، فقد ظهر بهذا الفرق أنه قسم آخر، والمشكك قد يكون مطلقاً كما سبق، وقد يكون بحسب النسبة إلى مبدأ واحد كقولنا : طبي للكتاب والمبضع والدواء، أو لانتسابه إلى غاية واحدة كقولنا: صحي للدواء والرياضة والفصد. وقد يكون إلى مبدأ وغاية واحدة كقولنا لجميع الأشياء : إنها الإلهية.
As for what is predicated by intensity and weakness, it is conceived in what admits intensity and weakness, like whiteness for ivory and snow; for it is not predicated of them with absolute equal univocality, but one of them is more intense in it than the other. As for 'animal' for Zaid, 'Amr, horse, and ox, no such variation occurs in any case. So it has become clear by this distinction that it is another division. And the equivocal (mushakkik) may be absolute as mentioned, or it may be according to relation to a single principle, like our saying 'medical' for the book, the lancet, and the medicine; or according to its relation to a single end, like our saying 'healthy' for the medicine, exercise, and venesection; and it may be to a single principle and end, like our saying for all things that they are divine.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of mushakkik (equivocal by priority and proportion). The author begins by contrasting it with mutawati' (univocal): mutawati' applies equally to all individuals, while mushakkik applies with varying intensity or relational priority. Examples: whiteness is more intense in snow than in ivory, so it is not purely univocal. However, 'animal' is truly univocal for Zaid, 'Amr, horse, and ox. The author then explains that mushakkik has three subtypes: (1) absolute, as previously mentioned; (2) according to relation to a single principle (e.g., 'medical' for book, lancet, medicine—all relate to medicine as a source); (3) according to relation to a single end (e.g., 'healthy' for medicine, exercise, venesection—all lead to health); and (4) to both a single principle and end (e.g., 'divine' for all things—they come from God and return to Him). This classification is crucial for understanding how words can share a meaning but not with equal intensity, a nuance often missed by beginners. For instance, in fiqh, the term 'salah' (prayer) is used for both the obligatory and supererogatory prayers with a shared meaning but different emphases.

Key Terms
المشكك
التواطؤ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best exemplifies mushakkik?
A) 'animal' for Zaid and horse
B) 'white' for snow and ivory
C) 'eye' for the organ and the spring of water
D) 'book' for a Quran and a notebook

Key Takeaway
Mushakkik is a vital concept for understanding how shared meanings can differ in intensity or relational priority, preventing confusion in Islamic legal and theological discourse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following terms as mutawati' or mushakkik, and explain your reasoning: (1) 'sawm' (fasting) for Ramadan fast and voluntary fast; (2) 'shirk' (polytheism) for major and minor shirk; (3) 'kitāb' (book) for the Quran and a fiqh manual. Use the criteria from the lesson.
?

Mutashabih: Resemblance and the Six Divisions of Names
Defines mutashabih and its three types, then lists six divisions of names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish mutashabih from other types of names and identify the three types of resemblance that justify transferred usage.

Core Idea
Mutashabih names link two things by resemblance, not shared meaning, and fall into three types based on the nature of resemblance.

In Islamic sciences, words are often used in multiple senses. Understanding how these senses are related—whether by shared meaning, pure homonymy, or resemblance—is crucial for interpreting texts correctly. This lesson introduces the concept of mutashabih (analogical names), where a term is transferred from its original meaning to a secondary one based on some resemblance, and then places it within a six-fold classification of all names.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the core distinction
Notice that mutashabih is not univocal (mutawati') because the two things do not share a single meaning; their definitions differ. For example, 'human' for a real human (rational mortal animal) and for a clay statue (artificial form).
The same name but completely different definitions.
2
Identify the three types of resemblance
The author lists three categories: (1) permanent essential attribute (e.g., form of a human); (2) relative non-essential attribute (e.g., 'principle' for endpoint and cause); (3) remote borrowed resemblance (e.g., 'dog' for star and animal, based on a tenuous similarity in following behavior). The third type is so weak it should be treated as pure homonymy (mushtarak).
Essential resemblance is stronger than relative or remote.
3
Understand the six-fold division of names
The passage ends by listing six types of names: mutabayina (distinct), mutaradifa (synonymous), mutawati'a (univocal), mushtaraka (homonymous), mushakkika (equivocal by priority), and mutashabiha (analogical). Each type captures a different relationship between a term and its referents.
This is a comprehensive taxonomy used in logical analysis.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
In usul al-fiqh, the word 'salah' is originally for the ritual prayer but is sometimes applied to supplication (du'a) due to resemblance in raising hands. This is a mutashabih usage of the 'relative non-essential' type, since the connection is based on a shared attribute (calling upon Allah) that is not intrinsic to the prayer's definition.
Recognizing the type of resemblance helps in correct legal interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الذي لا يجمعهما معنى واحد ولكن بينهما تشابه ما كالإنسان على من الطين بصورة الإنسان وعلى الإنسان الحقيقي، فليس هذا بالتواطؤ إذ يختلفان بالحد ، فحد هذا حيوان ناطق مائت، وحد ذلك شكل صناعي يحاكي به صورة حيوان ناطق مائت؛ وكذلك القائمة للحيوان وللسرير حدّه في أحدهما أنه عضو طبيعي يقوم عليه الحيوان ويمشي به، وفي الآخر أنه جسم صناعي مستدير في أسفل السرير ليقله ولكن نجد بينهما شبهاً في شكل أو حال. ومثل هذا الاسم يكون موضوعاً في أحدهما وضعاً متقدماً، ويكون منقولاً إلى الآخر، فإن أضيف إليهما سُمّي متشابه الاسم وإن أضيف إلى المتقدم منهما سُمّي موضوعاً، وإن أضيف إلى الأخير سُمّي منقولاً؛ ثم هذا الضرب من التشابه على ثلاثة أقسام : الأول أن يكون في صفة قارة ذاتية كصورة الإنسان. والثاني أن يكون في صفة إضافية غير ذاتية كاسم المبدأ لطرف الخط والعلة. والثالث أن يكون التشابه جارياً في أمر بعيد كالكلب لنجم مخصوص ولحيوان، إذ لا تشابه بينهما إلا في أمر بعيد مستعار لأن النجم رئي كالتابع للصورة التي كالإنسان، ثم وجد الكلب أتبع الحيوانات للإنسان فسمّي باسمه. ومثل هذا ينبغي أن يلحق بالمشترك المحض، فإنه لا عبرة بمثل هذا الاشتباه فقد صارت الأسامي بهذه القسمة ستة : متباينة، ومترادفة، ومتواطئة، ومشتركة، ومشككة ومتشابهة؛ لأن العقل إذا قسم الشيء إلى ستة أقسام فيحتاج إلى ستّ عبارات في التفهيم
As for that which does not share a single meaning but between them is some resemblance, like 'human' for a clay figure shaped like a human and for a real human—this is not univocal since their definitions differ: the definition of this is a rational mortal animal, and that is an artificial form imitating the form of a rational mortal animal. Similarly, 'leg' for an animal and for a bed: its definition in one is a natural limb upon which the animal stands and walks, and in the other is an artificial body placed at the bottom of the bed to support it, but we find between them a resemblance in form or state. Such a name is placed in one of them as a prior placement, and is transferred to the other. If it is predicated of both, it is called 'mutashabih al-ism' (analogous name); if predicated of the prior, it is called 'mawdu' (original); if predicated of the latter, it is called 'manqul' (transferred). Then this kind of resemblance has three divisions: first, it is in a permanent essential attribute, like the form of a human; second, it is in a relative non-essential attribute, like the name 'principle' for the endpoint of a line and the cause; third, the resemblance occurs in a remote matter, like 'dog' for a specific star and the animal, for there is no resemblance between them except in a remote borrowed matter, because the star was seen as following the image that is like a human, then the dog was found to be the most following of animals to humans, so it was named after it. Such a case should be attached to pure homonymy (mushtarak), for no attention is paid to such a resemblance. Thus names by this division become six: distinct (mutabayina), synonymous (mutaradifa), univocal (mutawati'a), homonymous (mushtaraka), equivocal by priority (mushakkika), and analogous (mutashabiha); because the intellect when dividing a thing into six parts needs six expressions for understanding.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage defines mutashabih (analogical/homonymous by resemblance). The author explains that two things may not share a single meaning but have a resemblance in form or state, like the word 'human' for a clay statue and a real human—their definitions are completely different. This is not univocality. The name originates for one ('mawdu') and is transferred ('manqul') to the other; when applied to both, it is called 'mutashabih'. The author then classifies resemblance into three types: (1) in a permanent essential attribute (e.g., form of a human); (2) in a relative non-essential attribute (e.g., 'principle' for a line's endpoint and for a cause); (3) in a remote borrowed resemblance (e.g., 'dog' for the star and the animal, based on the star's following of the human image). The third type is so weak that it should be treated as pure homonymy (mushtarak). The passage concludes with the six-fold division of names: mutabayina (distinct), mutaradifa (synonymous), mutawati'a (univocal), mushtaraka (homonymous), mushakkika (equivocal by priority), and mutashabiha (analogical). This is a comprehensive classification of the types of terms based on their relationships to their referents. For example, in Islamic law, the term 'witr' (odd) is used for the prayer and for the number, with a resemblance in the concept of oddness.

Key Terms
المتشابه (al-mutashābih) - analogous name
المنقول (al-manqūl) - transferred usage
الموضوع (al-mawḍūʿ) - original usage
المتواطئ (al-mutawāṭiʾ) - univocal
المشترك (al-mushtarak) - homonymous
المشكك (al-mushakkik) - equivocal by priority
المتباين (al-mutabāyin) - distinct
المترادف (al-mutarādif) - synonymous

Key Takeaway
The six-fold division of names—distinct, synonymous, univocal, homonymous, equivocal by priority, and analogous—provides a complete framework for analyzing the semantics of terms in Islamic logic and other sciences.

Distinguishing Apparent Synonymy: The Three Cases of Mutabayina
Author warns against mistaking distinct names for synonyms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify pairs of names of the same subject into three categories of mutabayina: subject-attribute, attribute-attribute, and attribute of attribute.

Core Idea
Not every pair of names for the same object are synonyms; different considerations make them distinct.

Have you ever thought that 'sword' and 'sharp' mean the same thing? In logic, they are distinct because 'sharp' includes an attribute. This lesson teaches you to spot the difference between true synonyms and apparent synonyms that are actually mutabayina.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and the names
Look for cases where the subject (the object) is the same but the names differ. In the passage, the author gives examples like 'sword' and 'sharp' both referring to the same weapon.
2
Determine the consideration for each name
Ask: does each name denote the subject alone, or the subject plus an attribute? For 'sword', it denotes the subject only; for 'sharp', it denotes the subject with the attribute of sharpness.
3
Classify into the three cases
The author lists three: (a) one name denotes subject, other denotes subject+attribute; (b) both denote different attributes of the same subject; (c) one denotes an attribute, the other denotes an attribute of that attribute.
4
Verify that they are not synonyms
Check if one name can replace the other without loss or addition of meaning. If not, they are mutabayina.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إرشاد إلى مزلة قدم في المتباينات ولا يخفى أن الموضوعات إذ تباينت مع تباين الحدود، فالأسامي متباينة متزايلة كالفرس والحجر، ولكن قد يتحد الموضوع ويتعدد الاسم بحسب اعتبارات، فيظن أنها مترادفة ولا تكون كذلك؛ فمن ذلك أن يكون أحد الاسمين له من حيث موضوعه والآخر من حيث له وصف كقولنا: سيف وصارم؛ فإن الصارم دلّ على موضوع موصوف بصفة الحدّة بخلاف السيف، ومن ذلك أن يدلّ كل واحد على وصف للموضوع الواحد كالصارم والمهند، فإن أحدهما يدل على حدته والآخر على نسبته، ومن ذلك أن يكون أحدهما بسبب وصف والآخر بسبب وصف الوصف كالناطق والفصيح. ومن المتباينة المشتق والمنسوب مع المشتق منه والمنسوب إليه كالنحو والنحوي والحديد والحداد والمال والمتموّل والعدل والعادل فإن العادل لو سمّي عدلاً كما سمّيت العدالة عدلاً كان ذلك من قبيل ما يقال باشتباه الاسم، ولكن غيّرت الصيغة وبقيت المادة والمعنى الأول وزيد فيه ما دلّ على زيادة المعنى فسمّي مشتقاً.
Guidance to a place of slipping of the foot concerning distinct names. And it is not hidden that when subjects are distinct with the distinctness of definitions, then the names are distinct and separate like 'horse' and 'stone'. But sometimes the subject is one and the name is multiple according to considerations, so one thinks they are synonyms but they are not. Among that is that one of the two names is based on its subject and the other based on an attribute, like our saying 'sword' and 'sharp'; for 'sharp' indicates a subject described with the attribute of sharpness, unlike 'sword'. And among that is that each indicates an attribute of the same subject, like 'sharp' and 'Indian-made'; for one indicates its sharpness and the other its provenance. And among that is that one is due to an attribute and the other due to an attribute of the attribute, like 'rational' and 'eloquent'. And among the distinct are the derivative and the relative along with their bases, like 'grammar' and 'grammarian', 'iron' and 'blacksmith', 'wealth' and 'wealthy', 'justice' and 'just'; for if 'just' were called 'justice' as justice was called 'justice', that would be a case of what is said by confusion of the name, but the form was changed while the material and original meaning remained, and there was added something indicating increase of meaning, so it is called a derivative.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage clarifies the concept of mubayinat (distinct names) and warns against mistaking them for synonyms. The author states that when subjects are distinct, names are distinct (e.g., horse and stone). However, when the subject is the same but names differ due to different considerations, one might mistakenly think they are synonyms. Three cases are given: (1) one name denotes the subject and the other denotes the subject with a specific attribute, e.g., 'sword' vs 'sharp' – both refer to the same object but 'sharp' adds the attribute of sharpness. (2) Both names denote different attributes of the same subject, e.g., 'sharp' and 'Indian-made' – one indicates sharpness, the other provenance. (3) One name denotes an attribute and the other an attribute of that attribute, e.g., 'rational' (an attribute of human) and 'eloquent' (an attribute of rationality). These are not true synonyms. Finally, the author discusses derivative (mushtaqq) and relative (mansub) names compared to their bases (e.g., grammarian from grammar). The change in form indicates a change in meaning, so they are distinct, not synonymous. For instance, in fiqh, 'zakat' (obligatory charity) and 'sadaqah' (voluntary charity) both involve giving but the legal ruling differs, so they are distinct.

Key Terms
المتباينة
المشتق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 'horse' and 'stone'
B) 'sword' and 'sharp'
C) 'rational' and 'eloquent'
D) 'grammar' and 'grammarian'

Key Takeaway
Names that refer to the same subject but differ in the consideration (subject only, attribute, or attribute of attribute) are mutabayina, not synonymous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a pair of terms from Islamic studies (e.g., 'salat' and 'du'a', 'hajj' and 'umrah') and determine if they are mutabayina or true synonyms. State your reasoning using the three cases.

Derivatives and Their Bases: Why 'Grammarian' Is Not a Synonym for 'Grammar'
Author explains derivative names and their distinctness from bases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a derivative (mushtaqq) name is distinct from its base and identify the added meaning indicated by the change in form.

Core Idea
A derivative name carries a new attribute, making it distinct from its base.

You might think that 'grammarian' just means 'grammar', but in logic, the change in form signals a new meaning—a person who practices grammar. This lesson teaches you to recognize derivatives and why they are mutabayina with their bases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base word and its derivative
For example, base 'nahw' (grammar) and derivative 'nahwi' (grammarian). Notice the change in form by adding the ya' nisba.
2
Determine the original meaning of the base
The base 'nahw' means the science of grammar.
3
Identify the added meaning in the derivative
The derivative 'nahwi' means a person associated with grammar, adding the attribute of relation or person.
4
Confirm they are mutabayina
Since the derivative includes an attribute not present in the base, they are not synonyms. If they were synonyms, one could replace the other without change in meaning, but 'grammarian' cannot replace 'grammar' in a sentence without distortion.

Musta'ar and Manqul: Temporary vs Permanent Word Transfer
Author introduces musta'ar and manqul as subdivisions of common words.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between musta'ar (metaphorical) and manqul (transferred) terms in Islamic texts and classify examples from usul al-fiqh accordingly.

Core Idea
Musta'ar is a borrowed metaphor; manqul is a permanent transfer that creates a new technical term.

Words often carry multiple meanings, but not all shifts are equal. The logicians here draw a crucial distinction: some uses are temporary loans, like calling the earth 'mother', while others become permanent fixtures, like 'salat' shifting from supplication to the ritual prayer. Recognizing this difference is essential for reading juristic and theological texts correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two divisions
The author introduces a seventh division of words that apply to multiple meanings: musta'ar (metaphorical) and manqul (transferred). Locate the definitions in the Arabic passage.
Both are subtypes of common words (mushtarak) but differ in permanence.
2
Understand the key distinction: permanence
Musta'ar is temporary—the word is 'borrowed' for a new meaning without making it permanent. Manqul is a permanent transfer: the word becomes fixed in the new sense while still used in the original, becoming a homonym.
Think of musta'ar like a loan; manqul like a sale.
3
Examine the examples given
The author provides 'mother' for the earth (musta'ar) and 'salat', 'hajj', 'kafir', 'fasiq' as manqul. Verify that these fit the definitions.
In usul al-fiqh, many religious terms are manqul from linguistic to legal meanings.
4
Apply to new terms
Given an unfamiliar term, determine if its shift is temporary (e.g., 'lion' for a brave man) or permanent (e.g., 'zakat' from purification to obligatory charity).
Check if the term's new meaning is established as a technical term in the discipline.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسمة السابعة : للفظ المطلق بالاشتراك على مختلفات: اعلم أن اللفظ المطلق على معان مختلفة ثلاثة أقسام: مستعارة ومنقولة ومخصوصة باسم المشترك. أما المستعارة فهي أن يكون اسم دالاً على ذات الشيء بالوضع ودائماً من أول الوضع إلى الآن ولكن يلقب به في بعض الأحوال لا على الدوام شيء آخر لمناسبته للأول على وجه من وجوه المناسبات، من غير أن يجعل ذاتياً للثاني وثابتاً عليه ومنقولاً إليه كلفظ الأم، فإنه موضوع للوالدة ويُستعار للأرض يقال: إنها أمّ البشر، بل ينقل إلى العناصر الأربعة فَتُسمّى أمهات على معنى أنها أصول والأم أيضاً أصل للولد. فهذه المعاني التي استعير لها لفظ الأم لها أسماء خاصة بها. وإنما تسمّى بهذه الأسامي في بعض الأحوال على طريق الاستعارة، وخصص باسم المستعار لأن العارية لا تدوم وهذا أيضاً يُستعار في بعض الأحوال. وأما المنقولة: فهي أن ينقل الاسم عن موضوعه إلى معنى آخر ويجعل اسماً له ثابتاً دائماً، ويستعمل أيضاً في الأول فيصير مشتركاً بينهما كاسم الصلاة والحج ولفظ الكافر والفاسق، وهذا يفارق المستعار بأنه صار ثابتاً في المنقول إليه دائماً
The seventh division: concerning the absolute word with commonality for different things. Know that the absolute word for different meanings is of three types: metaphorical (musta'ara), transferred (manqula), and that which is particularized by the name 'common' (mushtarak). As for the metaphorical: it is a name that indicates the essence of a thing by original placement and continuously from the first placement until now, but it is occasionally applied to something else due to its suitability to the first in some aspects, without making it essential for the second and permanent and transferred to it, like the word 'mother'; for it is placed for the parent and is metaphorically used for the earth—it is said that it is the mother of humanity; rather it is transferred to the four elements and they are called mothers in the sense that they are origins, and the mother is also an origin for the child. These meanings for which the word 'mother' is metaphorically used have their own special names. They are only called by these names occasionally in a metaphorical way, and it is specified with the name 'metaphorical' because a loan is not permanent, and this also is borrowed occasionally. As for the transferred: it is that the name is transferred from its original subject to another meaning and becomes a permanent name for it, and is also used for the first, so it becomes common between them, like the name 'prayer' (salat) and 'pilgrimage' (hajj) and the words 'unbeliever' (kafir) and 'sinner' (fasiq). This differs from the metaphorical in that it becomes permanent in the transferred to it.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage introduces a seventh division of terms: words used for multiple meanings. The author states that such words are of three types: (1) Musta'ar (metaphorical): a word that retains its original meaning but is used occasionally for something else due to resemblance, without becoming permanent. Example: 'mother' originally for the parent, metaphorically for the earth or the four elements. This is temporary, like a loan. (2) Manqul (transferred): a word that is permanently transferred from its original to a new meaning, and is used in both senses, thus becoming common (mushtarak) between them. Example: 'salat' originally meant 'supplication' but in Islamic law was transferred to the ritual prayer; similarly 'hajj' originally 'intention' but transferred to the pilgrimage; 'kafir' originally 'someone who covers' but transferred to 'disbeliever'; 'fasiq' originally 'something that deviates' but transferred to 'sinner'. (3) Mushtarak (common/homonymous): words that are shared between multiple meanings without priority. The contrast between musta'ar and manqul is that musta'ar does not permanently establish the new meaning, while manqul does. For example, in fiqh, the term 'wudu' (ritual washing) originally meant 'cleanliness' but was permanently transferred to the specific ablution; this is manqul, whereas calling a generous person 'ocean' is metaphorical (musta'ar).

Key Terms
المستعار (al-musta'ār) - metaphorical/borrowed term
المنقول (al-manqūl) - transferred term

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best exemplifies manqul (transferred) as defined in the passage?
A) Calling the earth 'mother' in poetry
B) The term 'salat' used for ritual prayer in Islamic law
C) Referring to a brave man as 'lion'
D) Using 'heart' for the center of a city

Key Takeaway
The critical difference between musta'ar and manqul is permanence: musta'ar is a temporary loan, manqul is a permanent transfer that creates a new technical meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each of the following Islamic terms as musta'ar (metaphorical) or manqul (transferred) based on the criteria in this lesson: (1) 'al-rahim' for the womb (originally meaning 'mercy'), (2) 'al-siraat' for the bridge over Hell (originally meaning 'path'), (3) 'al-wudu'' for the ritual ablution (originally meaning 'cleanliness'). For each, explain your reasoning.
?
Take the term 'zakat' (زكاة). Its linguistic meaning is 'purity' or 'growth'. In Islamic law, it refers to obligatory charity. Is this an example of musta'ar or manqul? Justify using the passage's definitions.

The Ten Categories
beginner
8 min
Essential, Necessary, and Accidental Attributes
Author defines three types of attributes with the example of man.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any attribute of a thing as essential, necessary, or accidental based on al-Ghazali's definitions and examples.

Core Idea
Essential attributes constitute the essence; necessary ones are inseparable in reality but not in concept; accidental ones can be separated entirely.

Al-Ghazali opens a critical discussion: when we describe something like 'man', not all attributes are equal. Some are part of its very definition, some are permanent but not defining, and some are temporary. This lesson unpacks these three categories which are foundational for constructing sound definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three attributes in the man example
Notice that al-Ghazali uses three predicates: 'this man is white', 'this man is animal', 'this man was born of a female'. Each represents a different type of attribute.
These are not arbitrary; they are chosen to illustrate the three categories.
2
Understand why whiteness is accidental
Whiteness can cease (e.g., if the man changes color) while he remains a man. It is not necessary for his essence.
Accidental attributes are separable in both existence and conception.
3
See why animality is essential
If you try to understand 'man' without understanding 'animal', you fail. Animality is necessarily included in the concept of man.
Essential attributes cannot be removed even in conception.
4
See why being born of a female is necessary
This attribute is inseparable in existence (no man is not born of a female), but it is not part of the definition of man—you can conceive of a man without thinking of his birth.
Necessary attributes are permanent in reality but not constitutive of the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسمة الرابعة في نسبة بعض المعاني إلى بعض: اعلم أنك تقول: هذا الإنسان أبيض، وهذا الإنسان حيوان، وهذا الإنسان ولدته أنثى، فقد حملت عليه البياض والحيوانية والولادة وجعلته موصوفاً بهذه الأوصاف الثلاثة، ونسبة هذه الثلاثة إليه متفاوتة: فإن البياض يتصوّر أن يبطل من الإنسان ويبقى إنساناً فليس وجوده شرطاً لإنسانيته ولنسم هذا عرضياً مفارقاً. وأما الحيوانية فضرورية للإنسان، فإنك إن لم تفهم الحيوان وامتنعت عن فهمه لم تفهم الإنسان بل مهما فهمت الإنسان قد فهمت حيواناً مخصوصاً، فكانت الحيوانية داخلة في مفهومك بالضرورة ويلقب هذا بلقب آخر للتمييز وهو الذاتي المقوم. وأما كونه مولوداً من أنثى وكونه متلوناً مثلاً فليس نسبته إليه كنسبة الحيوانية، إذ يجوز أن يحصل في العقل معنى الإنسان بحده وحقيقته مع الغفلة عن كونه مولوداً، ومن شرطه الامتناع عن كونه غير حيوان، وأما تميزه عن البياض فهو أن البياض قد يفارق وكونه مولوداً لا يفارقه قط، وكذلك كونه متلوناً بالجملة لا يفارقه وإن فارقه كونه أبيض على الخصوص، فالمتلونية ليست داخلة في ماهية الإنسان دخول الحيوانية، فنلخصص هذا القسم بلقب وهو اللازم؟ فإن الذاتي المقوم وإن كان أيضاً لازماً ولكن له خاصية التقويم، فيخصص اسم اللازم بهذا القسم. فقد استفدت من هذا التحقيق أنّ كلّ معنى ينسب إلى شيء، فإما أن يكون ذاتياً له مقوماً لذاته أي قوام ذاته به، وإما أن يكون غير ذاتي مقوم ولكنه لازم غير مفارق، وإما أن يكون لا ذاتياً ولا لازماً ولكن عَرَضياً. ولعلك تقول: الفرق بين العَرَضي المفارق وبين الذاتي واضح ولكن الفرق بين الذاتي المقوم وبين اللازم الذي ليس بمقوم ربما يشكل فهل لك معيار يرجع إليه؟ فنقول: المتكلمون سموا اللوازم توابع الذات، وربما سموها توابع الحدوث، حتى زعمت المعتزلة منهم أن توابع الحدوث لا تتعلّق بها قدرة القادر، ولكنها تتبع الحدوث، وربما مثلوا ذلك بتحيّز الجوهر، ولسنا نخوض فيه، والغرض إظهار معيار لإدراك الفرق بين الذاتي واللازم وله معياران: الأول: أن كل ما يلزم ولا يرتفع في الوجود إن أمكن أن يرتفع بالوهم والتقدير وبقي الشيء معه مفهوماً فهو لازم، فإنّا نفهم كون الإنسان إنساناً وكون الجسم جسماً وإن رفعنا من وهمنا اعتقاد كونهما مخلوقين مثلاً وكونهما مخلوقين لازم لهما، ولو رفعنا من وهمنا كون الإنسان حيواناً لم نقدر على فهم الإنسان فمن ضرورة فهم الإنسان أن لا يسلب الحيوانية وليس من ضرورته أن لا يسلب المخلوقية، فإذن ما لا يرتفع في الوجود والوهم جميعاً فهو ذاتي، وما يرتفع في الوجود والوهم فهو عَرَضي، وما يقبل الارتفاع في الوهم دون الوجود فهو لازم غير ذاتي، إلا أن هذا المعيار مع أنه كثير النفع في أغلب المواضع غير مُطّرد في الجميع فإن من اللوازم ما هو ظاهر اللزوم للشيء، بحيث لا يقدر على رفعه في الوهم أيضاً، فإن الإنسان يلازمه كونه متلوناً ملازمة ظاهرة لا يقدر الإنسان على رفعه في الوهم وهو لازم لا ذاتي، ولذلك إذا حددنا الإنسان لم يدخل فيه التلوّن مع أن الحد لا يخلو عن جميع الذاتيات المقومة كما سيأتي في كتاب الحدود. وكذلك كون كلّ عدد إما مساو لغيره أو مفاوت، فإنه لازم ليس بذاتي، وربما لا يقدر الإنسان على رفعه في الوهم نعم من اللوازم ما يقدر على رفعه ككون المثلث مساوي الزاويتين القائمتين فإنه لازم لا يعرف لزومه للمثلث بغير وسط بل بوسط، فلم يكن هذا مطّرداً فنعدل إلى المعيار الثاني عند العجز عن الأول ونقول: إن كلّ معنى إذا أحضرته في الذهن مع الشيء الذي شككت في لازم له أو ذاتي فإن لم يمكنك أن تفهم ذات الشيء إلا أن يكون قد فهمت له ذلك المعنى أولاً، كالحيوان والإنسان فإنك إذا فهمت ما الإنسان وما الحيوان فلا تفهم الإنسان إلا وقد فهمت أولاً أنه حيوان فاعلم أنه ذاتي. وإن أمكنك أن تفهم ذات الشيء دون أن تفهم المعنى أو أمكنك الغفلة عن المعنى بالتقدير، فاعلم أنه غير ذاتي. ثم إن كان يرتفع وجوده إما سريعاً كالقيام والقعود للإنسان أو بطيئاً ككونه شاباً فاعلم أنه عُرَضي مفارق، وإن كان لا يفارقه أصلاً ككون الزوايا من المثلث مساوية لقائمتين فهو لازم، ورُبَّ لازم للشخص كأزرق العين أو أسود البشرة في الزنجي، فهو لا يفارق في الوجود للإنسان الزنجي، فهو بالإضافة إلى ذلك الشخص لا يبعد أن يُسمّى لازماً، وإن كان لزومه بالاتفاق لا بالضرورة في الجنس، إذ يمكن وجود إنسان ليس كذلك، ولو أمكنت حيلة في إزالة زرقة العين
The fourth division is concerning the relation of some meanings to others. Know that you say: this man is white, this man is an animal, this man was born of a female. So you have predicated whiteness, animality, and birth upon him and made him described by these three attributes. The relation of these three to him is different: for whiteness can be conceived to cease from man while he remains man, so its existence is not a condition for his humanity, and let us name this 'accidental separable'. As for animality, it is necessary for man, for if you do not understand animal and refrain from understanding it, you do not understand man. Rather, whenever you understand man you have understood a specific animal. So animality is necessarily included in your concept, and this is given another label for distinction: 'essential constitutive'. As for his being born of a female and his being colored, for example, their relation to him is not like the relation of animality, for it is possible that the meaning of man with his definition and essence occurs in the mind while being heedless of his being born, while it is impossible for him to be other than an animal. As for its distinction from whiteness, it is that whiteness can separate, while his being born never separates from him, and similarly his being colored in general does not separate from him even if his being white in particular separates. So coloredness is not included in the quiddity of man as animality is included. So let us specify this section with a label: 'the necessary'. For the essential constitutive, although it is also necessary, has the property of constitution, so the name 'necessary' is specified for this section. So from this investigation you have learned that every meaning attributed to a thing is either essential to it, constitutive of its essence (i.e., its essence subsists by it), or non-essential constitutive but necessary and inseparable, or neither essential nor necessary but accidental. You might say: the difference between the accidental separable and the essential is clear, but the difference between the essential constitutive and the necessary that is not constitutive is perhaps confusing. So is there a criterion to refer to? So we say: the theologians called the necessary things 'followers of the essence', and sometimes they called them 'followers of origination', until the Mu'tazila among them claimed that the followers of origination are not subject to the power of the powerful, but they follow origination, and they gave the example of a substance's occupancy of space. We will not delve into that; the purpose is to present a criterion for perceiving the difference between the essential and the necessary. It has two criteria: The first: that everything that is necessary and does not cease in existence, if it is possible for it to cease in estimation and supposition and the thing remains conceptually, then it is necessary. For we understand man's being man and body's being body even if we remove from our estimation the belief that they are created, for example, while being created is necessary for them. But if we remove from our estimation man's being an animal, we cannot understand man. So it is necessary for understanding man that animality not be negated, while it is not necessary for him that createdness not be negated. So that which does not cease in both existence and estimation is essential; that which ceases in both existence and estimation is accidental; and that which admits cessation in estimation without existence is the necessary non-essential. However, this criterion, even though it is very useful in most places, is not universally applicable in all cases, for among the necessary things there is that whose necessity for the thing is so evident that one cannot remove it in estimation either. For man is accompanied by being colored with an evident accompaniment such that man cannot remove it in estimation, yet it is necessary and not essential. For that reason when we define man, we do not include color, though the definition lacks none of the essential constitutive attributes, as will come in the Book of Definitions. Similarly, every number is either equal to another or unequal, which is necessary and not essential, and perhaps one cannot remove it in estimation. Yes, among necessary things there is what can be removed, such as the triangle's being equal to two right angles, for it is necessary but its necessity for the triangle is not known without a middle term but through a middle term. So this was not universal, so we turn to the second criterion when unable to use the first, and we say: Every meaning that you bring to mind with the thing about which you doubt whether it is necessary or essential, if you cannot understand the essence of the thing except that you have first understood that meaning, like animal and man: for when you understand what man and what animal are, you do not understand man except after having first understood that he is an animal; then know that it is essential. If it is possible for you to understand the essence of the thing without understanding that meaning, or you can be heedless of the meaning by supposition, then know that it is non-essential. Then if its existence ceases, either quickly like standing and sitting for man, or slowly like his being young, then know that it is accidental separable. If it never separates at all, like the angles of a triangle being equal to two right angles, then it is necessary. And sometimes there is a necessary thing for an individual, like blue eyes or black skin for the Zanjī, it does not separate in existence from the Zanjī man, so in relation to that individual it is not far-fetched to be called necessary, even if its necessity is by coincidence not by necessity in the genus, for it is possible for a man to exist who is not like that, even if a trick could remove the blueness of the eye.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition section from al-Ghazali's Miyar al-Ilm. It divides attributes (meanings) predicated of a thing into three categories: essential (dhati), necessary (lazim), and accidental ('aradi). Al-Ghazali begins by illustrating with the example of man: whiteness is accidental (can cease while man remains man), animality is essential (necessary for understanding man), and being born of a female or being colored is necessary (inseparable but not part of the essence). He then formalizes: every meaning attributed to a thing is either constitutive of its essence (dhati muqawwim), or non-constitutive but necessary (lazim ghai muqawwim), or neither (aradi). The author anticipates a question: How to distinguish between the essential and the non-constitutive necessary? He first references theologians' terms but then provides two criteria. Criterion 1: If a meaning cannot be removed in both existence and conception, it is essential; if it can be removed in both, it is accidental; if it can be removed in conception but not in existence, it is necessary. However, he notes this criterion fails for some necessary attributes that are so obvious they cannot be removed even in conception (e.g., being colored for man). So he offers Criterion 2: If you cannot understand the thing without first understanding that meaning (e.g., animality for man), it is essential. If you can understand the thing without it, it is non-essential. Then subdivide: if it separates quickly or slowly, it is accidental; if it never separates (e.g., triangle's angles equaling two right angles), it is necessary. This is a careful logical classification essential for definition and demonstration.

ذاتي
عرضي
لازم

Key Takeaway
The essential attribute forms the core quiddity; the necessary attribute is inseparable in reality but not in concept; the accidental is separable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using al-Ghazali's three categories, classify the following attributes of 'fasting' (sawm): abstaining from food/drink, intention (niyyah), being during Ramadan, and traveling. For each, explain whether it is essential, necessary, or accidental based on whether it can be separated in conception and existence.

The Ten Categories
intermediate
10 min
The Two Criteria for Distinguishing Essential from Necessary
Author provides two criteria to distinguish essential from necessary attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply al-Ghazali's two criteria to determine whether an attribute is essential or necessary non-essential in any given concept.

Core Idea
Criterion 1 tests separability in conception vs existence; Criterion 2 tests whether the meaning is prior to understanding the essence.

After defining the three categories, al-Ghazali anticipates confusion between essential and necessary. He provides two powerful criteria, noting the first is not universal. This lesson teaches you to apply these criteria like a logician, using examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
State the confusion
Al-Ghazali acknowledges that the difference between accidental and essential is clear, but between essential and necessary it is subtle. The two criteria are designed to resolve this.
2
Understand Criterion 1: separability in existence and estimation (wahn)
An attribute that cannot be removed in existence but can be removed in estimation (conception) is necessary. If removable in both, it is accidental. If removable in neither, it is essential. For example, 'createdness' of a body: can be removed in estimation but not in existence → necessary. 'Animality' cannot be removed even in estimation → essential.
Al-Ghazali calls this 'evaluation by existence and estimation'.
3
Identify the limitation of Criterion 1
Criterion 1 fails for some necessary attributes that are so obvious that they cannot be removed even in estimation, like 'being colored' for man. Though necessary, it is not essential because it is not part of the definition. Similarly, 'every number is equal or unequal' is necessary but not removable in estimation.
This is why a second criterion is needed.
4
Apply Criterion 2: priority in understanding
If you cannot understand the essence of a thing without first understanding that attribute (e.g., animality for man), then the attribute is essential. If you can understand the essence without it, it is non-essential. Then, if it never separates in existence, it is necessary; if it separates quickly or slowly, it is accidental.
This criterion is more reliable but requires careful reflection.
5
Practice on a theological example
Consider God's attribute of 'being alive'. Can you conceive of God without being alive? The Ash'ari answer is no, because life is essential to God. But what about 'being powerful'? Some argue it is necessary but not essential.
This shows how the criteria are used in kalam.

متلوناً ملازمة ظاهرة لا يقدر الإنسان على رفعه في الوهم وهو لازم لا ذاتي، ولذلك إذا حددنا الإنسان لم يدخل فيه التلوّن مع أن الحد لا يخلو عن جميع الذاتيات المقومة كما سيأتي في كتاب الحدود. وكذلك كون كلّ عدد إما مساو لغيره أو مفاوت، فإنه لازم ليس بذاتي، وربما لا يقدر الإنسان على رفعه في الوهم نعم من اللوازم ما يقدر على رفعه ككون المثلث مساوي الزاويتين القائمتين فإنه لازم لا يعرف لزومه للمثلث بغير وسط بل بوسط، فلم يكن هذا مطّرداً فنعدل إلى المعيار الثاني عند العجز عن الأول ونقول: إن كلّ معنى إذا أحضرته في الذهن مع الشيء الذي شككت في لازم له أو ذاتي فإن لم يمكنك أن تفهم ذات الشيء إلا أن يكون قد فهمت له ذلك المعنى أولاً، كالحيوان والإنسان فإنك إذا فهمت ما الإنسان وما الحيوان فلا تفهم الإنسان إلا وقد فهمت أولاً أنه حيوان فاعلم أنه ذاتي. وإن أمكنك أن تفهم ذات الشيء دون أن تفهم المعنى أو أمكنك الغفلة عن المعنى بالتقدير، فاعلم أنه غير ذاتي. ثم إن كان يرتفع وجوده إما سريعاً كالقيام والقعود للإنسان أو بطيئاً ككونه شاباً فاعلم أنه عُرَضي مفارق، وإن كان لا يفارقه أصلاً ككون الزوايا من المثلث مساوية لقائمتين فهو لازم، ورُبَّ لازم للشخص كأزرق العين أو أسود البشرة في الزنجي، فهو لا يفارق في الوجود للإنسان الزنجي، فهو بالإضافة إلى ذلك الشخص لا يبعد أن يُسمّى لازماً، وإن كان لزومه بالاتفاق لا بالضرورة في الجنس، إذ يمكن وجود إنسان ليس كذلك، ولو أمكنت حيلة في إزالة زرقة العين
The fourth division is concerning the relation of some meanings to others. Know that you say: this man is white, this man is an animal, this man was born of a female. So you have predicated whiteness, animality, and birth upon him and made him described by these three attributes. The relation of these three to him is different: for whiteness can be conceived to cease from man while he remains man, so its existence is not a condition for his humanity, and let us name this 'accidental separable'. As for animality, it is necessary for man, for if you do not understand animal and refrain from understanding it, you do not understand man. Rather, whenever you understand man you have understood a specific animal. So animality is necessarily included in your concept, and this is given another label for distinction: 'essential constitutive'. As for his being born of a female and his being colored, for example, their relation to him is not like the relation of animality, for it is possible that the meaning of man with his definition and essence occurs in the mind while being heedless of his being born, while it is impossible for him to be other than an animal. As for its distinction from whiteness, it is that whiteness can separate, while his being born never separates from him, and similarly his being colored in general does not separate from him even if his being white in particular separates. So coloredness is not included in the quiddity of man as animality is included. So let us specify this section with a label: 'the necessary'. For the essential constitutive, although it is also necessary, has the property of constitution, so the name 'necessary' is specified for this section. So from this investigation you have learned that every meaning attributed to a thing is either essential to it, constitutive of its essence (i.e., its essence subsists by it), or non-essential constitutive but necessary and inseparable, or neither essential nor necessary but accidental. You might say: the difference between the accidental separable and the essential is clear, but the difference between the essential constitutive and the necessary that is not constitutive is perhaps confusing. So is there a criterion to refer to? So we say: the theologians called the necessary things 'followers of the essence', and sometimes they called them 'followers of origination', until the Mu'tazila among them claimed that the followers of origination are not subject to the power of the powerful, but they follow origination, and they gave the example of a substance's occupancy of space. We will not delve into that; the purpose is to present a criterion for perceiving the difference between the essential and the necessary. It has two criteria: The first: that everything that is necessary and does not cease in existence, if it is possible for it to cease in estimation and supposition and the thing remains conceptually, then it is necessary. For we understand man's being man and body's being body even if we remove from our estimation the belief that they are created, for example, while being created is necessary for them. But if we remove from our estimation man's being an animal, we cannot understand man. So it is necessary for understanding man that animality not be negated, while it is not necessary for him that createdness not be negated. So that which does not cease in both existence and estimation is essential; that which ceases in both existence and estimation is accidental; and that which admits cessation in estimation without existence is the necessary non-essential. However, this criterion, even though it is very useful in most places, is not universally applicable in all cases, for among the necessary things there is that whose necessity for the thing is so evident that one cannot remove it in estimation either. For man is accompanied by being colored with an evident accompaniment such that man cannot remove it in estimation, yet it is necessary and not essential. For that reason when we define man, we do not include color, though the definition lacks none of the essential constitutive attributes, as will come in the Book of Definitions. Similarly, every number is either equal to another or unequal, which is necessary and not essential, and perhaps one cannot remove it in estimation. Yes, among necessary things there is what can be removed, such as the triangle's being equal to two right angles, for it is necessary but its necessity for the triangle is not known without a middle term but through a middle term. So this was not universal, so we turn to the second criterion when unable to use the first, and we say: Every meaning that you bring to mind with the thing about which you doubt whether it is necessary or essential, if you cannot understand the essence of the thing except that you have first understood that meaning, like animal and man: for when you understand what man and what animal are, you do not understand man except after having first understood that he is an animal; then know that it is essential. If it is possible for you to understand the essence of the thing without understanding that meaning, or you can be heedless of the meaning by supposition, then know that it is non-essential. Then if its existence ceases, either quickly like standing and sitting for man, or slowly like his being young, then know that it is accidental separable. If it never separates at all, like the angles of a triangle being equal to two right angles, then it is necessary. And sometimes there is a necessary thing for an individual, like blue eyes or black skin for the Zanjī, it does not separate in existence from the Zanjī man, so in relation to that individual it is not far-fetched to be called necessary, even if its necessity is by coincidence not by necessity in the genus, for it is possible for a man to exist who is not like that, even if a trick could remove the blueness of the eye.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition section from al-Ghazali's Miyar al-Ilm. It divides attributes (meanings) predicated of a thing into three categories: essential (dhati), necessary (lazim), and accidental ('aradi). Al-Ghazali begins by illustrating with the example of man: whiteness is accidental (can cease while man remains man), animality is essential (necessary for understanding man), and being born of a female or being colored is necessary (inseparable but not part of the essence). He then formalizes: every meaning attributed to a thing is either constitutive of its essence (dhati muqawwim), or non-constitutive but necessary (lazim ghai muqawwim), or neither (aradi). The author anticipates a question: How to distinguish between the essential and the non-constitutive necessary? He first references theologians' terms but then provides two criteria. Criterion 1: If a meaning cannot be removed in both existence and conception, it is essential; if it can be removed in both, it is accidental; if it can be removed in conception but not in existence, it is necessary. However, he notes this criterion fails for some necessary attributes that are so obvious they cannot be removed even in conception (e.g., being colored for man). So he offers Criterion 2: If you cannot understand the thing without first understanding that meaning (e.g., animality for man), it is essential. If you can understand the thing without it, it is non-essential. Then subdivide: if it separates quickly or slowly, it is accidental; if it never separates (e.g., triangle's angles equaling two right angles), it is necessary. This is a careful logical classification essential for definition and demonstration.

Key Terms
ذاتي
لازم
عرضي
معيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali, why is the first criterion not universally applicable?
A) Because some necessary attributes are so obvious they cannot be removed in estimation.
B) Because all essential attributes can be removed in estimation.
C) Because accidental attributes are always separable.
D) Because the criterion is too complex.

Key Takeaway
When the first criterion fails, use the second criterion: if the meaning must be understood before the essence, it is essential; otherwise, subdivide into necessary or accidental based on existential separability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the attribute 'being aware' for God. Apply Criterion 2: Is it possible to conceive of God without being aware? If you answer yes, then it is non-essential. Then check if it can separate in existence (for God, it cannot, so it would be necessary). If you answer no, then it is essential. Discuss which position aligns with Ash'ari and Mu'tazili views.

The Ten Categories

The Hierarchy of Genera and Species
Author describes the necessary endpoints in the hierarchy of essential genera and species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the ascending and descending order of genera and species and identify the highest genus and ultimate species.

Core Idea
Essential predicates form a tree with a highest genus and ultimate species.

In this passage, the author explains the necessary ordering of essential predicates (al-dhatiyat). Genera and species ascend until they reach the genus of genera (jins al-ajnas), which is the highest genus. They descend until they reach the ultimate species (al-naw' al-akhir), below which are only individuals and accidents. This hierarchy is fundamental for understanding how definitions work in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two directions
The author describes two directions: ascending (tasā'ud) and descending (tanazzul). Note that the essential predicates can move upward to more universal genera or downward to more specific species.
2
Locate the highest genus
The highest genus is 'jins al-ajnas' (the genus of genera). There is no genus above it. It is also called a 'maqula' (category).
3
Locate the ultimate species
The ultimate species is 'al-naw' al-akhir' (the final species). Below it are only individuals (ashkhas) and accidents (a'rad). You cannot go further in essential predicates.
4
Understand the necessity of the endpoints
The author argues that the chain must have endpoints: the highest genus cannot be surpassed by essential predicates, and the ultimate species cannot be subdivided by essences. Beyond these, only accidents and concomitants can be added.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن هذه الذاتيات التي هي أجناس وأنواع تترتب متصاعدة إلى أن تنتهي إلى جنس الأجناس، وهو الجنس العالي الذي ليس فوقه جنس، وتترتب متنازلة حتى تنحط إلى النوع الأخير الذي إن نزلت منه انتهت إلى الأشخاص والأعراض، ولا بد من انتهاء الجنس العالي في التنازل إلى نوع أخير إذ ليس يخرج عن النهاية، ولا بد من ارتفاع النوع الأخير في التصاعد إلى جنس عال لا يمكن مجاوزته إلَّا بذكر العوارض واللوازم فأما الذاتيات فتنتهي لا محالة والأنواع الأخيرة كثيرة، والأجناس العالية التي هي أعلى الأجناس زعم المنطقيون أنها عشرة واحد جوهر وتسعة أعراض وهي : الكم والكيف والمضاف والأين ومتى والوضع وله وأن يفعل وأن ينفعل). فالجوهر مثل قولنا : إنسان وحيوان وجسم، والكم مثل قولنا : ذو ذراع وذو ثلاثة أذرع ، والكيف : مثل قولنا: أبيض وأسود، : والمضاف مثل قولنا : ضعف ونصف وابن ،وأب والأين: مثل قولنا : في السوق وفي الدار ومتى مثل قولنا في زمان كذا ووقت كذا والوضع مثل قولنا: متكئ وجالس، وأن يفعل : مثل قولنا : يحرق ويقطع، وأن ينفعل : مثل قولنا : يحترق ويتقطع، وله : مثل قولنا : مُتنعّل ومُتطلس ومتسلّح. وقد تجتمع العشرة في شخص واحد في سياق كلام واحد كما تقول : إن الفقيه الفلاني الطويل الأسمر ابن فلان الجالس في بيته في سنة كذا يعلم ويتعلم وهو مُتطلس. فهذه
Then know that these essential predicates that are genera and species ascend in order until they end at the genus of genera, which is the highest genus above which there is no genus, and they descend in order until they fall to the ultimate species, from which if you descend, you end at individuals and accidents. It is necessary that the highest genus in its descent ends at an ultimate species, because it does not exit from infinity, and it is necessary that the ultimate species in its ascent rises to a highest genus that cannot be transcended except by mentioning accidents and concomitants. As for essential predicates, they necessarily end. The ultimate species are many. The highest genera that are the highest genera – the logicians claim that they are ten: one substance and nine accidents: quantity, quality, relation, where, when, position, possession, action, passion. Substance is like our saying: human, animal, body. Quantity is like our saying: possessing a cubit, possessing three cubits. Quality is like our saying: white, black. Relation is like our saying: double, half, son, father. Where is like our saying: in the market, in the house. When is like our saying: in such a time, such a moment. Position is like our saying: reclining, sitting. Action is like our saying: burning, cutting. Passion is like our saying: being burned, being cut. Possession is like our saying: wearing sandals, wearing a taylasan, being armed. And the ten can combine in one person in one context, as you say: 'That certain faqih, tall, brown-skinned, son of so-and-so, sitting in his house in such a year, teaches and learns, and he is wearing a taylasan.' So these [examples].
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage classifies the essential predicates (dhatiyat) that form the tree of genera and species. The author explains the necessary ordering: ascending from the ultimate species to the highest genus (al-jins al-'ali), and descending from the highest genus to the ultimate species (al-naw' al-akhir). The highest genus cannot be transcended with essential predicates; only accidents and concomitants can be added beyond it. The logicians (mainly Aristotle and his followers) identify ten highest genera (al-maqulat al-ashr), which are the most universal categories. They consist of one substance (jawhar) and nine accidents (a'rad). Substance examples: human, animal, body. The nine accidents are: quantity (e.g., length in cubits), quality (e.g., color), relation (e.g., fatherhood), where (e.g., location), when (e.g., time), position (e.g., sitting), possession (e.g., wearing something), action (e.g., cutting), and passion (e.g., being cut). The author gives concrete examples from everyday language, and shows that all ten can be predicated of a single person in one sentence: the faqih (substance), tall and brown (quality), son of so-and-so (relation), sitting (position), in his house (where), in such a year (when), teaches (action), learns (passion), wearing a taylasan (possession). This illustrates how categories structure predication.

Key Terms
جنس الأجناس (jins al-ajnas): The highest genus, the most universal category that cannot be subsumed under a higher genus. [Note: Stable term; synonymous with 'maqula' (category) in this context.]
نوع أخير (naw' akhir): Ultimate species; the lowest species under which only individuals fall. [Note: Stable.]
مقولات عشر (maqulat 'ashr): The ten categories: substance and nine accidents. [Note: Standard Aristotelian categories adopted in Islamic logic; stable.]
جوهر (jawhar): Substance; the independent quiddity that does not inhere in another. [Note: In kalam, 'jawhar' often means 'atom'; here it means substance in Peripatetic sense.]
عرض ('arad): Accident; what inheres in a substance. [Note: In logic, 'arad' is a category; in kalam it often means an attribute that perishes.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what lies below the ultimate species (al-naw' al-akhir)?
A) Other genera
B) Individuals and accidents
C) The highest genus
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
The hierarchy of essential predicates necessarily ends at a unique highest genus (jins al-ajnas) and a unique ultimate species (naw' akhir).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Trace the hierarchy of essential predicates for the term 'insan' (human). Start from the highest genus (jawhar) and descend to the ultimate species (insan). List the intermediate genera (e.g., jism, hayawan).
?
Explain why the highest genus cannot be transcended by essential predicates, using the example of 'substance' (jawhar).

The Ten Categories (al-Maqulat al-'Ashr)
Author lists the ten highest genera and gives examples for each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the ten categories (substance and nine accidents) and classify examples from everyday language.

Core Idea
All predication falls into ten highest genera: one substance and nine accidents.

In this passage, the author lists the ten highest genera (al-maqulat al-'ashr) that logicians have established. These are the most universal categories of predication: substance (jawhar) and nine accidents. Understanding these categories helps in analyzing the logical structure of statements, which is essential for defining terms and constructing valid arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the ten categories
The ten categories are: Substance, Quantity, Quality, Relation, Where, When, Position, Possession, Action, Passion. Substance is independent; the other nine are accidents.
2
Study the examples for each category
For each category, the author gives Arabic examples: Substance (human, animal, body), Quantity (cubit), Quality (white, black), Relation (father, son), Where (in the market), When (in such a time), Position (sitting, reclining), Possession (wearing sandals), Action (cutting), Passion (being cut). Note how each example predicates a different aspect.
3
Analyze the compound example
The passage shows all ten categories combined in one sentence about a faqih: substance (faqih), quality (tall, brown), relation (son of), position (sitting), where (in house), when (in year), action (teaches), passion (learns), possession (wearing taylasan). Quantity is missing but can be added. This shows categories are not exclusive.
4
Practice classifying new predicates
Take any sentence describing an object and assign each descriptive phrase to a category. This trains your ability to see the logical structure behind everyday language.

It is necessary that the highest genus in its descent ends at an ultimate species, because it does not exit from infinity, and it is necessary that the ultimate species in its ascent rises to a highest genus that cannot be transcended except by mentioning accidents and concomitants. As for essential predicates, they necessarily end. The ultimate species are many. The highest genera that are the highest genera – the logicians claim that they are ten: one substance and nine accidents: quantity, quality, relation, where, when, position, possession, action, passion.
