The Ten Categories
beginner
7 min
Hierarchy of Genera and Species
Explanation of the hierarchy of genera and species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the highest genus and the lowest species in any chain of essential predicates and explain why 'existent' is not a genus.

Core Idea
Highest genus tops the chain; lowest species bottoms it; 'existent' is above both.

This passage establishes the hierarchical structure of essential predicates (genera and species). You will learn how they ascend to a highest genus and descend to a lowest species, and why the chain must have endpoints. This foundation is crucial for understanding how definitions are structured in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two directions of arrangement
Notice that the author describes two movements: ascending (الصعود) to the highest genus, and descending (النزول) to the lowest species. This is the backbone of the hierarchy.
Essential predicates (الذاتيات) are those that enter the definition of a thing.
2
Define the endpoints
The highest genus (الجنس العالي) has no genus above it; the lowest species (النوع الأخير) has no species below it—only individuals (أشخاص) and accidents (أعراض). Understand that these are necessary boundaries.
3
Understand why endpoints are necessary
The author argues that without an endpoint, the chain would be infinite, which is not permitted. The ascending chain stops at the highest genus, and the descending chain stops at the lowest species.
This is a logical requirement to avoid infinite regress.
4
Recognise that 'existent' is not a genus
The author mentions that 'existent' (الموجود) is more general than the highest genus but is not itself a genus. Why? Because it does not supply a differentia that distinguishes one genus from another; it cuts across all categories.
This is a key point: 'existent' is an accident in the sense of being a concomitant, not an essential predicate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن هذه الذاتيات التي هي أجناس وأنواع تترتب متصاعدة إلى أن تنتهي إلى جنس الأجناس، وهو الجنس العالي الذي ليس فوقه جنس، وتترتب متنازلة حتى تنحط إلى النوع الأخير الذي إن نزلت منه انتهت إلى الأشخاص والأعراض، ولا بد من انتهاء الجنس العالي في التنازل إلى نوع أخير إذ ليس يخرج عن النهاية، ولا بد من ارتفاع النوع الأخير في التصاعد إلى جنس عال لا يمكن مجاوزته إلَّا بذكر العوارض واللوازم فأما الذاتيات فتنتهي لا محالة والأنواع الأخيرة كثيرة، والأجناس العالية التي هي أعلى الأجناس زعم المنطقيون أنها عشرة واحد جوهر وتسعة أعراض وهي : الكم والكيف والمضاف والأين ومتى والوضع وله وأن يفعل وأن ينفعل. فالجوهر مثل قولنا : إنسان وحيوان وجسم، والكم مثل قولنا : ذو ذراع وذو ثلاثة أذرع، والكيف : مثل قولنا: أبيض وأسود، والمضاف مثل قولنا : ضعف ونصف وابن وأب، والأين: مثل قولنا : في السوق وفي الدار، ومتى مثل قولنا في زمان كذا ووقت كذا، والوضع مثل قولنا: متكئ وجالس، وأن يفعل : مثل قولنا : يحرق ويقطع، وأن ينفعل : مثل قولنا : يحترق ويتقطع، وله : مثل قولنا : مُتنعّل ومُتطلس ومتسلّح. وقد تجتمع العشرة في شخص واحد في سياق كلام واحد كما تقول : إن الفقيه الفلاني الطويل الأسمر ابن فلان الجالس في بيته في سنة كذا يعلم ويتعلم وهو مُتطلّس. فهذه أجناس الموجودات والألفاظ الدالة عليها بواسطة آثارها في النفس، أعني ثبوت صورها في النفس وهي العلم بها فلا معلوم إلا وهو داخل في هذه الأقسام، ولا لفظ إلا وهو دال على شيء من هذه الأقسام فأما الأعمّ من جميعها فهو الموجود، وقد ذكرنا أنه ليس جنساً وينقسم بالقسمة الأولى إلى الجوهر والعرض، والعرض ينقسم إلى هذه الأقسام التسعة، فيكون المجموع عشرة، ولهذا مزيد تفصيل وتحقيق سيساق إليك في كتاب أقسام الوجود وأحكامه، فإنه بحث عن انقسام الموجودات، والله أعلم.
Then know that these essential [predicates] which are genera and species are arranged ascending until they reach the genus of genera, which is the highest genus above which there is no genus, and they are arranged descending until they descend to the lowest species, from which if you descend further you reach individuals and accidents. It is necessary that the highest genus in descending ends at a lowest species, since it does not exit from the end, and it is necessary that the lowest species in ascending rises to a highest genus that cannot be exceeded except by mentioning accidents and concomitants. As for the essential [predicates], they necessarily end, and the lowest species are many. The highest genera which are the highest genera, the logicians claimed that they are ten: one substance and nine accidents: quantity, quality, relation, place, time, position, possession, action, passion. Substance like our saying: human, animal, body. Quantity like our saying: having an arm, having three arms. Quality like our saying: white, black. Relation like our saying: double, half, son, father. Place like our saying: in the market, in the house. Time like our saying: in such a time, in such a moment. Position like our saying: reclining, sitting. Action like our saying: burns, cuts. Passion like our saying: burns, is cut. Possession like our saying: wearing sandals, wearing a taylasan, armed. These ten can come together in one person in one context of speech, as you say: 'Indeed the such-and-such jurist, the tall, the brown-skinned, son of so-and-so, sitting in his house in such a year, learns and teaches, and he is wearing a taylasan.' So these are the genera of existents and the words indicating them by means of their effects in the soul, I mean the establishment of their forms in the soul, which is knowledge of them. So there is no known thing except that it falls within these divisions, and no word except that it indicates something from these divisions. As for the most general of them all, it is 'existent', and we have mentioned that it is not a genus. It divides by the first division into substance and accident. Accident divides into these nine divisions, so the total becomes ten. This has more detail and verification that will be presented to you in the book 'Divisions of Existence and Their Rulings', for it is a discussion of the division of existents. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage discusses the hierarchical arrangement of genera and species and introduces the ten categories (al-maqūlāt al-'ashr) that form the highest genera. The author explains that essential predicates (al-dhātiyyāt) ascend to a highest genus (jins al-ajnās) and descend to a lowest species (al-naw' al-ākhir). The lowest species cannot descend further without reaching individuals and accidents. The highest genera, according to logicians, are ten: one substance (jawhar) and nine accidents (a'rāḍ). The author then gives examples for each category from everyday and scholarly contexts. Substance is exemplified by 'human', 'animal', 'body'. Quantity by 'having an arm', quality by 'white' and 'black', relation by 'double', 'half', 'son', 'father'. Place by 'in the market', time by 'in such a time', position by 'reclining' or 'sitting', action by 'burns', passion by 'is burnt', possession by 'wearing sandals' or 'wearing a taylasan' (a cloak). Then the author gives an example where all ten categories apply to a single individual: a jurist who is tall, brown-skinned, son of someone, sitting in his house in a certain year, learning and teaching, wearing a taylasan. This demonstrates that the categories are not arbitrary but cover all aspects of descriptions. The passage concludes that these ten categories encompass all known existents except for 'existent' itself, which is not a genus but is divided first into substance and accident. This section serves to ground logical definitions in the ontological structure of reality.

The Ten Categories
intermediate
10 min
The Ten Categories and Their Application
Enumeration and explanation of the ten categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the ten categories, classify any description into its appropriate category, and verify that no known existent falls outside them.

Core Idea
Everything knowable falls under one of ten categories – one for 'what it is' and nine for 'how it is'.

Now that you understand the hierarchy, the author lists the ten highest genera (المقولات العشر) that encompass all existents. This lesson will help you memorise the list, understand each category through Islamic examples, and see how a single individual can instantiate all ten.

Guided Path
1
Memorise the ten categories
The author states that the highest genera are ten: one substance (الجوهر) and nine accidents (الأعراض). List them: الكم, الكيف, المضاف, الأين, متى, الوضع, له, أن يفعل, أن ينفعل.
Use the mnemonic: 'Substance plus the nine accidents'.
2
Understand each category with examples
For each accident category, the author gives an example. For instance, الكم (quantity) is 'having an arm', الكيف (quality) is 'white' or 'black', المضاف (relation) is 'double' or 'father', etc. Read the examples carefully and connect them to the category.
The examples are from everyday life and from Islamic scholarly contexts.
3
Practise categorising descriptions
Take a description like 'a tall scholar wearing a turban'. Identify: 'tall' is quality, 'wearing turban' is possession (له). Try to assign each attribute to one of the ten categories.
This skill is essential for logical analysis of definitions.
4
Apply the categories to the jurist example
The author gives a comprehensive example: a tall brown-skinned jurist, son of someone, sitting in his house in a certain year, learns and teaches, wearing a taylasan. Identify which category each part falls into. Notice that all ten categories are covered, showing the comprehensiveness of the system.
The phrase 'مُتطلس' (wearing a taylasan) is an example of the category 'له' (possession).

The Ten Categories

The Ten Categories of Existence
Author summarizes the division of existents into ten categories: substance and nine accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the ten categories of existents and explain why 'existent' is not a genus.

Core Idea
All existents are either substance or accident; 'existent' itself is not a genus.

This lesson distills the foundational division of all existents into ten categories. The author presents a clear summary: everything that exists and every meaningful word falls under substance or one of nine accidents. Understanding this classification is essential for navigating Islamic logic and metaphysics, as it provides a universal framework for defining terms and analyzing reality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the most universal concept
Find 'al-mawjud' (the existent) and note that it is not a genus, even though it encompasses all categories.
A genus must have a differentia; 'existent' has no differentia because it applies to everything.
2
Trace the first division
Recognize that 'al-mawjud' is divided into substance (jawhar) and accident (arad). Substance does not inhere in a subject; accident does.
3
Account for the subdivision of accident
Accident is further divided into nine categories, making a total of ten categories.
The names of the nine accidents are detailed elsewhere, but the key point is the number.
4
Connect categories to language and knowledge
Understand that every known thing (ma'lum) and every word (lafz) falls under one of these ten divisions.
This shows the comprehensive scope of the categorical scheme.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أجناس الموجودات والألفاظ الدالة عليها بواسطة آثارها في النفس، أعني ثبوت صورها في النفس وهي العلم بها فلا معلوم إلا وهو داخل في هذه الأقسام، ولا لفظ إلا وهو دال على شيء من هذه الأقسام فأما الأعمّ من جميعها فهو الموجود، وقد ذكرنا أنه ليس جنساً وينقسم بالقسمة الأولى إلى الجوهر والعرض، والعرض ينقسم إلى هذه الأقسام التسعة، فيكون المجموع عشرة، ولهذا مزيد تفصيل وتحقيق سيساق إليك في كتاب أقسام الوجود وأحكامه، فإنه بحث عن انقسام الموجودات، والله أعلم.
The genera of existents and the words that indicate them by means of their effects in the soul — I mean the establishment of their forms in the soul, which is knowledge of them. So no known thing is outside these divisions, and no word except that it indicates something from these divisions. As for the most universal of all, it is existence [al-mawjud], and we have mentioned that it is not a genus. It is divided by the first division into substance and accident. Accident is divided into these nine divisions, so the total becomes ten. There is further detail and verification that will be presented to you in the book on the divisions of existence and its rulings, for it is a discussion of the division of existents. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a summary of the ten categories discussed earlier. The author states that existents and the words signifying them are all encompassed by these categories. The most universal concept is 'existent' (al-mawjud), but it is not a genus. The primary division is into substance (jawhar) and accident (arad). Accident itself is subdivided into nine categories, making a total of ten. The author refers the reader to another work for a more detailed treatment. This division is fundamental in Islamic logic and metaphysics, as it provides a comprehensive classification of all that exists and all meaningful terms.

The 55 Technical Terms of Natural Philosophy
Author lists fifty-five technical terms used in natural philosophy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize the fifty-five technical terms of natural philosophy into conceptual groups and identify their relevance to Islamic philosophical discourse.

Core Idea
Natural philosophy has its own precise vocabulary; mastering it unlocks the entire discipline.

This passage presents a comprehensive list of fifty-five terms that form the backbone of natural philosophy (al-tabi'iyyat). The author compiled them as a memorization anchor for students. Understanding these terms and their relationships is essential before engaging with any substantive discussions in physics, cosmology, and related fields.

Guided Path
1
Read the list carefully
Read through the fifty-five terms in the Arabic source. Note any that are familiar from logic or grammar.
The list begins with fundamental pairs like form and matter.
2
Group terms into thematic categories
Identify categories such as: matter-form (الصورة، الهيولى، المادة), elements (النار، الهواء، الماء، الأرض), celestial (الفلك، الكوكب، الشمس، القمر), motion-time (الحركة، الدهر، الزمان، الآن), qualities (الحرارة، البرودة، الرطوبة، اليبوسة), and abstract (العدم، المكان، الخلا).
Some terms may fit multiple categories.
3
Reflect on the term's role in natural philosophy
For each group, consider why every term is necessary for describing the physical world. How do they interrelate?
For example, motion requires time and place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لفظاً القسم في الطبيعيات للملائكة الثالث : هو المستعمل في الطبيعيات ونذكر منها خمسة وخمسين وهي : الصورة والهيولى والموضوع ،والمحمول، والمادة، والعنصر، والاسطقس والركن والطبيعة والطبع والجسم والجوهر، والعرض، والنار، والهواء، والماء والأرض والعالم، والفلك والكوكب، والشمس والقمر، والحركة، والدهر ،والزمان والآن ،والمكان والخلا، والملا، والعدم، والسكون ،والسرعة والبطء والاعتماد ،والميل ،والخفة والثقل، والحرارة والرطوبة والبرودة واليبوسة والخشن ،والأملس والصلب واللين والرخو، والمشفّ ،والتخلخل والاجتماع ،والتجانس والمداخل، والمتصل، والاتحاد، والتتالي، والتوالي.
Lexically, the division in natural philosophy for the [third]? is that which is used in natural philosophy, and we mention fifty-five of them. They are: form, matter, subject, predicate, material, element, element, pillar, nature, natural disposition, body, substance, accident, fire, air, water, earth, world, sphere, planet, sun, moon, motion, time indefinite, time, moment, place, void, plenum, non-existence, rest, speed, slowness, inclination, tendency, lightness, heaviness, heat, moisture, cold, dryness, roughness, smoothness, hardness, softness, sponginess, rarefaction, condensation, homogeneity, interlocking, continuous, union, succession, sequence.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a lexical enumeration of technical terms used in the science of natural philosophy (al-tabi'iyyat). The author introduces it with a heading indicating a division within natural philosophy, possibly for the third part or for something else (the OCR shows 'للملائكة' which is unclear). He states that there are fifty-five terms and proceeds to list them. The list begins with fundamental concepts like form (sura), matter (hayula), subject, predicate, material, element, then moves to specific natural substances like the four elements (fire, air, water, earth), celestial bodies (sphere, planet, sun, moon), and then various physical and metaphysical concepts such as motion, time, place, void, plenum, qualities, and relations. This list serves as a memorization anchor for students of natural philosophy, providing the essential terminology for the discipline.

The Multiple Meanings of Substance (Jawhar)
Al-Ghazali distinguishes the various meanings of substance and accident and critiques the mutakallimun's restrictive usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four meanings of substance (jawhar) as distinguished by al-Ghazali and explain which meanings apply to God, hyle, and form.

Core Idea
Substance is not a single meaning; it shifts across disciplines and contexts.

Al-Ghazali opens his discussion of the categories with a detailed analysis of the term 'substance' (jawhar). He shows that this key term is used in at least four distinct senses, ranging from the broad philosophical notion of an independent existent to the more restricted sense of a corporeal substratum. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for reading later logical and theological texts, where authors often shift between meanings without warning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining characteristic of substance
Find the phrase 'exists in a substrate but not in a subject'. Understand that substrate (mahall) is where it inheres, subject (mawdu') is what it is predicated of. A substance is not predicated of anything else.
2
List the four meanings of substance
From the passage: first (any independent existent like whiteness), second (enduring subject), third (subject of change), fourth (corporeal substratum). Note that the First Principle (God) is substance in all except the third.
The third meaning involves succession of opposites, implying change, which cannot apply to God.
3
Apply the meanings to specific entities
Check hyle (substance in third and fourth), form (substance in fourth only). The mutakallimun restrict substance to the indivisible atom; consider why al-Ghazali rejects this restriction.
Al-Ghazali's broader usage includes non-corporeal entities.
4
Recognize the critique of terminological disputes
Al-Ghazali dismisses disputes over names after meanings are clarified as 'the habit of those of limited capacity.'
This is a methodological point: clarity of meaning supersedes terminological preference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون في محل لا يتقوم المحلّ دونه بالفعل فإنه وإن كان في محل فليس في موضوع، فكلّ موجود إن كان كالبياض والحرارة والحركة والعلم فهو جوهر بالمعنى الأول، والمبدأ الأول جوهر بالمعاني كلها إلا بالوجه الثالث وهو تعاقب الأضداد . نعم قد يتحاشى عن إطلاق لفظ الجوهر عليه تأدباً من حيث الشرع، والهيولى جوهر بالمعنى الرابع والثالث، وليس جوهراً بالمعنى الثاني، والصورة جوهر بالمعنى الرابع وليس جوهراً بالمعنى الثاني والثالث، والمتكلمون يخصصون اسم الجوهر بالجوهر الفرد المتحيّز الذي لا ينقسم، ويسمون المنقسم جسماً لا جوهراً، وبحكم ذلك يمتنعون عن إطلاق اسم الجوهر على المبدأ الأول عزّ لكلّ وجلّ، والمشاحة في الأسماء بعد إيضاح المعاني دأب ذوي القصور. العَرَضُ : اسم مشترك فيقال لكلّ موجود في محل عرض، ويقال: عرض موجود في موضوع، ويقال : عرض للمعنى الكلّي المفرد المحمول على كثيرين حملا غير مقوم، وهو العَرَض الذي قابلناه بالذاتي في كتاب مقدمات القياس. ويقال : عَرَض لكلّ معنى موجود للشيء خارج عن طبعه، ويقال : عَرَض لكلّ معنى يحمل على الشيء لأجل وجوده في آخر يفارقه، ويقال: عرض لكلّ معنى وجوده في أول الأمر لا يكون ؛ فالصورة عرض بالمعنى الأول فقط، وهو الذي يعنيه المتكلم إذا ما قابله بالجوهر والأبيض، أي الشيء ذو البياض الذي يحمل على الثلج والجص والكافور ليس هو عَرَضاً بالوجه الأول والثاني، وهو عَرَضٌ بالوجه الثالث، وذلك لأن هذا الأبيض الذي هو نوع محمول غير مقوم، وهو جوهر ليس في موضوع ولا محلّ، فالبياض هو الحال في محل وموضوع، والبياض لا يحمل على الثلج فلا ثلج بياض، بل يقال : أبيض، ومعناه أنه شيء ذو أبيض فلا يكون هذا حملاً مقوماً. وحركة الحجر إلى أسفل عرض بالوجه الأول والثاني والثالث، وليس عَرَضاً بالوجه الرابع والخامس والسادس، بل حركته إلى هذه الوجوه، وحركة القاعد في السفينة عَرَض بالوجه السادس فوق عَرَض بجميع والرابع .
It exists in a substrate (mahall) such that the substrate is not constituted without it actually, for though it is in a substrate, it is not in a subject. So every existent that is like whiteness, heat, motion, and knowledge is a substance in the first meaning. The First Principle is a substance in all meanings except the third aspect, which is the succession of opposites. Yet one might refrain from applying the term substance to it out of reverence from the perspective of the Law. Hyle is a substance in the fourth and third meanings, but not a substance in the second meaning. Form is a substance in the fourth meaning, but not a substance in the second and third meanings. The mutakallimun restrict the name substance to the indivisible atom that occupies space, and they call the divisible a body, not a substance. By this they refrain from applying the term substance to the First Principle. Dispute over names after clarifying meanings is the habit of those of limited capacity. 'Accident' is a homonym: it is said of every existent in a substrate; it is said of an existent in a subject; it is said of a universal, simple meaning predicated of many without constituting predication—this is the accident we contrasted with the essential in the book Muqaddimat al-Qiyas. It is said of every meaning existing for a thing outside its nature; it is said of every meaning predicated of a thing due to its existence in another that is separate from it; it is said of every meaning whose existence is not in the first instance. So form is an accident in the first meaning only, and this is what the mutakallim means when he contrasts it with substance. The white object, i.e., the thing having whiteness that is predicated of snow, lime, and camphor, is not an accident in the first and second aspects; it is an accident in the third aspect. This is because this white, which is a non-constitutive predicated species, is a substance not in a subject or substrate. Whiteness is what inheres in a substrate and subject, and whiteness is not predicated of snow—snow is not whiteness—rather it is said 'white,' meaning it is a thing having whiteness, so this is not a constitutive predication. The motion of a stone downward is an accident in the first, second, and third aspects, but not an accident in the fourth, fifth, and sixth aspects; rather its motion is per these aspects. The motion of a seated person in a ship is an accident in the sixth aspect ... and the fourth.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a detailed elaboration on the multiple meanings of the terms 'substance' (jawhar) and 'accident' ('arad) as used by philosophers and theologians. Al-Ghazali begins by describing a characteristic of substance: it exists in a substrate (mahall) but not in a subject (mawdu'), meaning it does not inhere in another thing as a subject. For example, whiteness, heat, motion, and knowledge are substances in the first sense (they are qualities or forms that exist in a body but are not themselves bodies). He then notes that the First Principle (God) is substance in all senses except the third (which is the succession of opposites, implying change). However, out of reverence, one may avoid calling God 'substance' due to religious scruples. Hyle (prime matter) and form (surah) are substances in specific senses. Al-Ghazali then criticizes the mutakallimun (theologians) who restrict 'substance' to the indivisible atom (jawhar fard) and deny it to composite bodies and to God. He dismisses such terminological disputes after meanings have been clarified. Next, he lists six meanings of 'accident' (He defines each: 1) an existent in a substrate; 2) an existent in a subject; 3) a universal predicated non-constitutively; 4) an attribute external to the nature of the thing; 5) a property predicated because it exists in something else; 6) a meaning that does not exist in the first instance. He then applies these meanings to examples: form is an accident only in the first meaning; a white object (al-abyad) is not an accident in the first two but is an accident in the third; the motion of a stone downward is an accident in the first three but not the rest; and the motion of a seated person in a ship is an accident in the sixth aspect. The passage clarifies how terms shift across disciplines.

Key Terms
جوهر
محل
موضوع
هيولى
صورة

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali, which of the following is true about hyle (hayula)?
A) It is a substance in the first meaning.
B) It is a substance in the second meaning.
C) It is a substance in the third and fourth meanings.
D) It is not a substance at all.

Key Takeaway
Substance is a homonym whose precise meaning must be determined by context; al-Ghazali identifies four senses applicable to different entities.

The Six Meanings of Accident ('Arad) and Critique of Terminological Disputes
Al-Ghazali distinguishes the various meanings of substance and accident and critiques the mutakallimun's restrictive usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the six meanings of accident ('arad) as defined by al-Ghazali and apply them to examples like the white object and motion.

Core Idea
'Accident' is not just the opposite of substance; it has six distinct logical and ontological senses.

After clarifying substance, al-Ghazali turns to its counterpart, accident ('arad). He notes that 'arad is a homonym (ism mushtarak) with at least six distinct meanings, ranging from the ontological (existent in a substrate) to the logical (non-constitutive universal predicate). He then applies these meanings to concrete examples, showing how a single thing can be accident in some senses but not others. This precision helps avoid confusion when reading texts across different disciplines.

Guided Path
1
List the six meanings of accident
From the passage: 1) existent in a substrate; 2) existent in a subject; 3) universal non-constitutive predicate; 4) attribute outside the thing's nature; 5) predicate due to existence in another; 6) meaning not existing in the first instance.
The first two are ontological, the third is logical (used in logic books like Muqaddimat al-Qiyas), and the last three are more specific.
2
Distinguish the first three meanings from the rest
The first two involve inherence (in a substrate or subject), while the third is a matter of predication. Note that the third meaning is what logicians often mean by 'accidental predicate'.
3
Apply the meanings to examples
Form is accident only in the first meaning. The white object (al-abyad) is accident only in the third meaning (it is a non-constitutive predicate). Whiteness itself is accident in the first meaning. Motion of a stone is accident in the first three meanings.
The white object is a substance in itself but is predicated non-constitutively, so it is an accident in the logical sense.
4
Recognize the critique of terminological squabbles
Al-Ghazali's multiplicity of meanings undermines the mutakallimun's attempt to rigidly define accident. He implies that debating which meaning is 'correct' after clarification is pointless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Defining al-Nihaya (The End) in Logic
Definition of the term 'end' in logic and mathematics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of النهاية (the end) and identify its genus and differentia.

Core Idea
The end is the ultimate limit beyond which nothing of its kind exists.

In Islamic logic, mathematical terms like 'end' are used as tools in theological and philosophical arguments. Understanding the precise definition of النهاية is essential for grasping arguments about finitude and infinity, especially in the Kalam cosmological proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is النهاية (al-nihāya). Look for the colon after it in the Arabic passage.
2
Extract the genus
The genus is 'غاية' (ghāya) meaning a limit or extremity, specifically of a thing possessing quantity (كمية).
Think of any quantitative thing, like a line or a number, having an end point.
3
Identify the differentia
The differentia is that nothing of the same kind (من نوعه) exists beyond it. This distinguishes an end from a mere boundary.
For example, the end of a line has no further line beyond it.
4
Test the definition's completeness
Check if the definition includes all ends (e.g., end of a line, end of a number) and excludes things that are not ends (e.g., a point not at a limit).
This definition is complete for mathematical ends.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثالث : ما يستعمل في الرياضيات. ولمّا لم نتكلم في كتاب «تهافت الفلاسفة» على الرياضيات اقتصرنا من هذه الألفاظ على قدر يسير، وقد يدخل بعضها في الإلهيات والطبيعيات في الأمثلة والاستشهادات، وهي ستة ألفاظ : النهاية، وما لا نهاية، والنقطة، والخط، والسطح، والبعد. النهاية : هي غاية ما يصير الشيء ذو الكمية إلى حيث لا يوجد وراءه شيء من نوعه. ما لا نهاية له : هو كمّ ذو أجزاء كثيرة بحيث لا يوجد شيء خارج عنه وهو من نوعه، وبحيث لا ينقضي. النقطة : ذات غير منقسمة ولها وضع وهي نهاية الخط. الخط : هو مقدار لا يقبل الانقسام إلا من جهة واحدة وهو نهاية السطح. السطح : مقدار يمكن أن يحدث فيه قسمان متقاطعان على قوائم، وهو نهاية الجسم. البعد : هو كلّ ما يكون بين نهايتين غير متلاقيتين ويمكن الإشارة إلى جهته، شأنه أنه ومن يتوهم أيضاً فيه نهايات من نوع تينك النهايتين، والفرق بين البعد والمقادير الثلاثة أنه قد يكون بعد خطي من غير خط، وبعد سطحي من غير سطح.
The third section: What is used in mathematics. Since we did not discuss in the book 'The Incoherence of the Philosophers' concerning mathematics, we have limited ourselves to a small number of these terms, and some of them may enter into metaphysics and physics in examples and citations. They are six terms: the end, the infinite, the point, the line, the surface, and the dimension. The end: it is the limit to which a thing possessing quantity reaches such that nothing of its kind exists beyond it. The infinite: it is a quantity with many parts such that there is nothing outside it of its kind, and it is not exhausted. The point: an indivisible entity having position, and it is the end of the line. The line: a magnitude that is divisible only in one direction, and it is the end of the surface. The surface: a magnitude in which two intersecting divisions can occur at right angles, and it is the end of the body. The dimension: it is whatever exists between two non-meeting endpoints and can be pointed to in its direction, and it is such that one may also imagine within it endpoints of the same type as those two endpoints. The difference between dimension and the three magnitudes is that there may be a linear dimension without a line, and a surface dimension without a surface.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage defines six mathematical terms that al-Ghazali uses in logical examples throughout his work. He introduces the section by noting that he does not need to discuss mathematics in detail here, but these terms appear in metaphysical and physical examples. The definitions are precise: النهاية (the end) is the ultimate limit of a quantity beyond which nothing of its kind exists. ما لا نهاية (the infinite) is a quantity that is inexhaustible and has no external limit of its kind. النقطة (the point) is indivisible and has position; it is the end of a line. الخط (the line) is divisible only in one dimension (length) and is the end of a surface. السطح (the surface) is divisible in two dimensions (length and width) and is the end of a body. البعد (dimension) is a more general concept: the distance between two non-meeting endpoints, and it can exist without a line (e.g., distance between two points) or without a surface (e.g., distance between two lines). This distinction is crucial for understanding how logical concepts map onto mathematical intuitions. The terms are standard in the Islamic philosophical tradition and are used here as tools for logical analysis, not as an independent mathematical treatise.

Key Terms
النهاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) A magnitude divisible only in one direction, and it is the end of a surface.
D) A quantity with many parts such that nothing exists outside it of its kind.

Key Takeaway
The end (النهاية) is the ultimate boundary of a quantity beyond which no other of the same kind exists.

Defining Mā Lā Nihāya (The Infinite) in Logic
Definition of the term 'infinite' in logic and mathematics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of ما لا نهاية (the infinite) and distinguish it from the finite.

Core Idea
The infinite is an inexhaustible quantity with nothing of its kind outside it.

The concept of the infinite is central to Islamic theological debates, especially regarding the creation of the universe. Al-Ghazali's definition here clarifies what is meant by 'infinite' in logical contexts, setting the stage for arguments about actual infinites.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is ما لا نهاية له (mā lā nihāya lahu), literally 'that which has no end'.
2
Extract the genus
The genus is 'كم' (kamm) meaning quantity. So the infinite is a type of quantity.
It is a measurable thing, but without an external boundary of its kind.
3
Identify the differentiae
Two differentiae: 1) having many parts (ذو أجزاء كثيرة), 2) nothing outside it of its kind, and 3) it is not exhausted (لا ينقضي).
These three features together define the infinite in contrast to the finite end.
4
Compare with النهاية
Notice that the infinite is defined via absence: no external limit, while the end was defined via presence of a limit. This contrast is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثالث : ما يستعمل في الرياضيات. ولمّا لم نتكلم في كتاب «تهافت الفلاسفة» على الرياضيات اقتصرنا من هذه الألفاظ على قدر يسير، وقد يدخل بعضها في الإلهيات والطبيعيات في الأمثلة والاستشهادات، وهي ستة ألفاظ : النهاية، وما لا نهاية، والنقطة، والخط، والسطح، والبعد. النهاية : هي غاية ما يصير الشيء ذو الكمية إلى حيث لا يوجد وراءه شيء من نوعه. ما لا نهاية له : هو كمّ ذو أجزاء كثيرة بحيث لا يوجد شيء خارج عنه وهو من نوعه، وبحيث لا ينقضي. النقطة : ذات غير منقسمة ولها وضع وهي نهاية الخط. الخط : هو مقدار لا يقبل الانقسام إلا من جهة واحدة وهو نهاية السطح. السطح : مقدار يمكن أن يحدث فيه قسمان متقاطعان على قوائم، وهو نهاية الجسم. البعد : هو كلّ ما يكون بين نهايتين غير متلاقيتين ويمكن الإشارة إلى جهته، شأنه أنه ومن يتوهم أيضاً فيه نهايات من نوع تينك النهايتين، والفرق بين البعد والمقادير الثلاثة أنه قد يكون بعد خطي من غير خط، وبعد سطحي من غير سطح.
The third section: What is used in mathematics. Since we did not discuss in the book 'The Incoherence of the Philosophers' concerning mathematics, we have limited ourselves to a small number of these terms, and some of them may enter into metaphysics and physics in examples and citations. They are six terms: the end, the infinite, the point, the line, the surface, and the dimension. The end: it is the limit to which a thing possessing quantity reaches such that nothing of its kind exists beyond it. The infinite: it is a quantity with many parts such that there is nothing outside it of its kind, and it is not exhausted. The point: an indivisible entity having position, and it is the end of the line. The line: a magnitude that is divisible only in one direction, and it is the end of the surface. The surface: a magnitude in which two intersecting divisions can occur at right angles, and it is the end of the body. The dimension: it is whatever exists between two non-meeting endpoints and can be pointed to in its direction, and it is such that one may also imagine within it endpoints of the same type as those two endpoints. The difference between dimension and the three magnitudes is that there may be a linear dimension without a line, and a surface dimension without a surface.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage defines six mathematical terms that al-Ghazali uses in logical examples throughout his work. He introduces the section by noting that he does not need to discuss mathematics in detail here, but these terms appear in metaphysical and physical examples. The definitions are precise: النهاية (the end) is the ultimate limit of a quantity beyond which nothing of its kind exists. ما لا نهاية (the infinite) is a quantity that is inexhaustible and has no external limit of its kind. النقطة (the point) is indivisible and has position; it is the end of a line. الخط (the line) is divisible only in one dimension (length) and is the end of a surface. السطح (the surface) is divisible in two dimensions (length and width) and is the end of a body. البعد (dimension) is a more general concept: the distance between two non-meeting endpoints, and it can exist without a line (e.g., distance between two points) or without a surface (e.g., distance between two lines). This distinction is crucial for understanding how logical concepts map onto mathematical intuitions. The terms are standard in the Islamic philosophical tradition and are used here as tools for logical analysis, not as an independent mathematical treatise.

Key Terms
ما لا نهاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali, which of the following is NOT a characteristic of the infinite (ما لا نهاية)?
A) It has many parts.
B) There is nothing outside it of its kind.
C) It is exhausted after a finite time.
D) It is not exhausted.

Key Takeaway
The infinite is a quantity that is unbounded and inexhaustible, with no external limit of its kind.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the Kalam argument, the universe's past is considered. Explain why an 'actual infinite' of past events is deemed impossible according to al-Ghazali's definition.
?
Contrast the definition of the infinite (ما لا نهاية) with the definition of the end (النهاية). How does each help in arguments about finitude?

Defining al-Nuqṭa (The Point) in Logic
Definition of the term 'point' in logic and mathematics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of النقطة (the point) and identify its two essential properties.

Core Idea
A point is an indivisible entity with position, serving as the end of a line.

The point is a foundational concept in geometry and logic. Al-Ghazali defines it with two key attributes: indivisibility and position. Understanding this definition is important for grasping how logical definitions are constructed and how geometric terms are used in Islamic philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is النقطة (al-nuqṭa), the point.
2
Extract the first differentia: indivisibility
The point is 'ذات غير منقسمة' (an indivisible entity). It cannot be divided into smaller parts.
This distinguishes it from a line or surface which are divisible.
3
Extract the second differentia: having position
The point has 'وضع' (position), meaning it occupies a location in space.
This distinguishes it from a pure number or a 'nothing'.
4
Note the relational property
The point is also the end of a line (نهاية الخط). This connects it to the definition of line.
This shows how definitions build on each other.

Defining al-Khaṭṭ (The Line) in Logic
Definition of the term 'line' in logic and mathematics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of الخط (the line) and identify its divisibility property and its role as the end of a surface.

Core Idea
A line is a magnitude divisible only in one dimension (length) and is the end of a surface.

The line is a one-dimensional magnitude. Al-Ghazali defines it by its divisibility property (only in one direction) and its relational role as the boundary of a surface. This definition helps in understanding how geometric entities are hierarchically nested.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is الخط (al-khaṭṭ), the line.
2
Extract the genus
The genus is 'مقدار' (miqdār) meaning magnitude or measure.
A line is a type of continuous quantity.
3
Identify the differentia: one-dimensional divisibility
The line is divisible only in one direction (لا يقبل الانقسام إلا من جهة واحدة), i.e., length. It has no width or depth.
This distinguishes it from a surface (divisible in two directions) and a body (three directions).
4
Note the relational property
The line is the end of a surface (نهاية السطح). Just as a point is the end of a line, a line bounds a surface.
This shows the hierarchy: point → line → surface → body.

Source Text

Defining al-Saṭḥ (The Surface) in Logic
Definition of the term 'surface' in logic and mathematics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of السطح (the surface) and explain its two-dimensional divisibility and its role as the end of a body.

Core Idea
A surface is a two-dimensional magnitude divisible by two perpendicular lines, and it is the end of a body.

The surface is the next geometric entity after the line. Al-Ghazali defines it by its two-dimensional divisibility and its function as the boundary of a three-dimensional body. This definition completes the hierarchy of magnitudes used in logical examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is السطح (al-saṭḥ), the surface.
2
Extract the genus
The genus is again 'مقدار' (magnitude), as for the line.
So both line and surface are magnitudes, differing in dimensions.
3
Identify the differentia: two-dimensional divisibility
The surface is divisible in two directions (يمكن أن يحدث فيه قسمان متقاطعان على قوائم). It can be divided by two intersecting perpendicular lines.
This means it has length and width, but no depth.
4
Note the relational property
The surface is the end of the body (نهاية الجسم). Every three-dimensional body is bounded by surfaces.
This completes the chain: point bounds line, line bounds surface, surface bounds body.

Defining al-Bu'd (The Dimension) in Logic
Definition of the term 'dimension' in logic and mathematics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of البعد (the dimension) and explain how it differs from the three magnitudes (line, surface, body).

Core Idea
A dimension is the distance between two non-meeting endpoints; it can exist without a line or surface.

The concept of dimension (البعد) is more general than line, surface, or body. Al-Ghazali distinguishes it by noting that a dimension can be linear without an actual line, or surface-like without an actual surface. This nuanced definition is important for logical reasoning about abstract quantities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is البعد (al-bu'd), dimension or distance.
2
Extract the genus and essential properties
The dimension is described as 'كل ما يكون بين نهايتين غير متلاقيتين' (everything that exists between two non-meeting endpoints). So it is a relation between two endpoints.
Unlike a line, it does not require a continuous magnitude.
3
Understand the differentia: possibility of imaginary endpoints
One can imagine endpoints of the same type within the dimension (يتوهم أيضاً فيه نهايات من نوع تينك النهايتين). This means a dimension can be subdivided in thought.
4
Note the key distinction from the three magnitudes
Unlike a line, surface, or body, a dimension may be linear without a line (بعد خطي من غير خط) or surface-like without a surface (بعد سطحي من غير سطح). Examples: the distance between two points is a linear dimension even if no line is drawn; the gap between two parallel lines is a surface dimension without an actual surface.
This shows that dimension is more abstract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثالث : ما يستعمل في الرياضيات. ولمّا لم نتكلم في كتاب «تهافت الفلاسفة» على الرياضيات اقتصرنا من هذه الألفاظ على قدر يسير، وقد يدخل بعضها في الإلهيات والطبيعيات في الأمثلة والاستشهادات، وهي ستة ألفاظ : النهاية، وما لا نهاية، والنقطة، والخط، والسطح، والبعد. النهاية : هي غاية ما يصير الشيء ذو الكمية إلى حيث لا يوجد وراءه شيء من نوعه. ما لا نهاية له : هو كمّ ذو أجزاء كثيرة بحيث لا يوجد شيء خارج عنه وهو من نوعه، وبحيث لا ينقضي. النقطة : ذات غير منقسمة ولها وضع وهي نهاية الخط. الخط : هو مقدار لا يقبل الانقسام إلا من جهة واحدة وهو نهاية السطح. السطح : مقدار يمكن أن يحدث فيه قسمان متقاطعان على قوائم، وهو نهاية الجسم. البعد : هو كلّ ما يكون بين نهايتين غير متلاقيتين ويمكن الإشارة إلى جهته، شأنه أنه ومن يتوهم أيضاً فيه نهايات من نوع تينك النهايتين، والفرق بين البعد والمقادير الثلاثة أنه قد يكون بعد خطي من غير خط، وبعد سطحي من غير سطح.
The third section: What is used in mathematics. Since we did not discuss in the book 'The Incoherence of the Philosophers' concerning mathematics, we have limited ourselves to a small number of these terms, and some of them may enter into metaphysics and physics in examples and citations. They are six terms: the end, the infinite, the point, the line, the surface, and the dimension. The end: it is the limit to which a thing possessing quantity reaches such that nothing of its kind exists beyond it. The infinite: it is a quantity with many parts such that there is nothing outside it of its kind, and it is not exhausted. The point: an indivisible entity having position, and it is the end of the line. The line: a magnitude that is divisible only in one direction, and it is the end of the surface. The surface: a magnitude in which two intersecting divisions can occur at right angles, and it is the end of the body. The dimension: it is whatever exists between two non-meeting endpoints and can be pointed to in its direction, and it is such that one may also imagine within it endpoints of the same type as those two endpoints. The difference between dimension and the three magnitudes is that there may be a linear dimension without a line, and a surface dimension without a surface.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage defines six mathematical terms that al-Ghazali uses in logical examples throughout his work. He introduces the section by noting that he does not need to discuss mathematics in detail here, but these terms appear in metaphysical and physical examples. The definitions are precise: النهاية (the end) is the ultimate limit of a quantity beyond which nothing of its kind exists. ما لا نهاية (the infinite) is a quantity that is inexhaustible and has no external limit of its kind. النقطة (the point) is indivisible and has position; it is the end of a line. الخط (the line) is divisible only in one dimension (length) and is the end of a surface. السطح (the surface) is divisible in two dimensions (length and width) and is the end of a body. البعد (dimension) is a more general concept: the distance between two non-meeting endpoints, and it can exist without a line (e.g., distance between two points) or without a surface (e.g., distance between two lines). This distinction is crucial for understanding how logical concepts map onto mathematical intuitions. The terms are standard in the Islamic philosophical tradition and are used here as tools for logical analysis, not as an independent mathematical treatise.

البعد

Key Takeaway
A dimension is an abstract distance between two endpoints that can be imagined without requiring a concrete line or surface.

The Ten Categories: The Classification of Existence in Islamic Logic
Author lists the ten categories and explains their correspondence to mental examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the ten categories in Arabic and explain their relationship to mental knowledge and external reality.

Core Idea
The ten categories classify all existence into substance and nine accidents, mirroring the structure of reality in the mind.

This passage marks the beginning of the first art of logic after discussing terms and propositions: the categories of existence. The author emphasizes that the categories are not arbitrary labels but correspond to real divisions in the external world, and our knowledge of them is a mental image that faithfully represents those divisions. This lesson helps you memorize the standard list and understand the epistemological grounding that makes them reliable tools for reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recall the purpose of categories
The author states that the categories are divisions of existence (أقسام الوجود). Note that they are 'ten types in themselves' — they are not merely mental constructs but reflect real distinctions in external reality.
2
Understand the correspondence between knowledge and the known
Knowledge is described as an 'example matching the known' (مثال مطابق للمعلوم), like a form or engraving that corresponds to the thing. This establishes that our mental categories are trustworthy because they derive from the actual structure of existence.
3
List the ten categories from the passage
Identify the ten Arabic terms in order: الجوهر (substance), الكم (quantity), الكيف (quality), المضاف (relation), الأين (where/place), متى (when/time), الوضع (position), له (possession), أن يفعل (action), أن ينفعل (passion). Commit these to memory.
The first category is substance; the remaining nine are accidents.
4
Recognize the non-disputable nature of technical terms
The author concludes that after we grasp the meanings, 'there is no dispute about the words.' This means that once the intended meaning of each category is understood, debates about terminology are secondary. Focus on concepts, not labels.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الأول في أقسام الوجود وهي عشرة أنواع في أنفسها، ثم يكون أمرها في النفس أعني : العلم بها أيضاً عشرة متباينة، فإن العلم معناه مثال مطابق للمعلوم كالصورة والنقش الذي هو مثال الشيء، فيكون لها عشر عبارات إذ الألفاظ تابعة للآثار الثابتة في النفس المطابقة للأشياء الخارجية، وتلك الألفاظ هي : الجوهر والكم والكيف والمضاف والأين ومتى والوضع وله وأن يفعل وأن ينفعل. فهذه العبارات أوردها المنطقيون، ونحن نكشف معنى كلّ واحدٍ منها وبعد الإحاطة بالمعنى فلا مشاحة في الألفاظ.
The first art: On the divisions of existence. They are ten types in themselves. Then their affair in the soul—i.e., knowledge of them—is also ten distinct things. For knowledge is an example matching the known, like a form or an engraving that is an example of a thing. So they have ten expressions, since words follow the impressions established in the soul that match external things. Those expressions are: substance, quantity, quality, relation, where, when, position, possession, action, and passion. These are the expressions that the logicians have presented, and we will uncover the meaning of each one. After grasping the meaning, there is no dispute about the words.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage defines the first art of the book, which is the study of the ten categories of existence. The author states that the categories themselves are ten distinct types. Correspondingly, knowledge of them in the soul is also ten distinct 'examples' (amthal). He explains that knowledge is an image that corresponds to the known object, like a picture or engraving that represents a thing. Words (expressions) derive from these mental impressions that match external objects. Then he lists the ten categories: jawhar (substance), kamm (quantity), kayf (quality), idafa (relation), ayn (where—place), mata (when—time), wad‘ (position), laka (possession—having a state like 'he has a sword'), an yaf‘al (action), an yanfa‘il (passion—being acted upon). This list is standard in Aristotelian logic. The author notes that logicians have presented these expressions, and after we grasp the meanings, we should not dispute about the words themselves, since technical terms can be agreed upon.

Substance and Accident: Definitions and Terminological Shifts
Author defines substance and accident and distinguishes terminologies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the philosophers' and mutakallimun's definitions of substance and accident using the criteria of essence change.

Core Idea
Substance is what exists not in a subject; accident is what inheres, but the divide depends on how you define 'subject'.

This passage defines the foundational categories of substance and accident. The author clarifies that the terms are ambiguous across disciplines. He uses the example of water transforming into air to show that some properties change the essence (like the form of waterness) while others (like heat) do not. This distinction is key to understanding how philosophers and theologians classify the same entity differently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Locate the definitions of substance given for philosophers (لا في موضوع) and for mutakallimun (ليس في محل). Note the parallel definitions for accident.
The philosopher's 'subject' (موضوع) is the proximate self-subsisting locus; the mutakallim's 'locus' (محل) is simply the thing in which an accident inheres.
2
Extract the genus and differentia for each
For philosophers: genus = existent (موجود), differentia = not in a subject. For mutakallimun: genus = existent, differentia = not in a locus. This gives two different definitions of substance.
The genus is the same but the differentia changes, leading to different classifications of form and matter.
3
Analyse the water example
Notice how waterness (صورة المائية) differs from heat: when water turns to air, the answer to 'what is it?' changes from water to air, so waterness is essential. Heat can leave and the substance remains water, so heat is accidental.
This is the key test: does the property affect the essence (ذات) or not?
4
Understand the terminological shift
Both groups call waterness an 'accident' in their own terminology, but for philosophers it is a substance because it is not in a subject, while for mutakallimun it is an accident because it is in a locus.
The author says terminology (اصطلاح) should not be disputed; the real distinction is the essential vs accidental change.
5
Apply the definition to prime matter
Prime matter (هيولى) is the common substrate underlying change (e.g., between water and air). For philosophers it is a substance; for mutakallimun it would be an accident if in a locus, but they do not typically use this concept.
This shows how philosophical ontology includes prime matter as substance, while kalam does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول في الجوهر
اعلم أن الموجود ينقسم بنوع من القسمة إلى الجوهر والعرض، واسم كل من الجوهر والعَرَض مشترك كما سبق، ولكنّا نعني الآن من جملتها شيئاً واحداً فنريد بالجوهر الموجود لا في موضوع ونريد بالموضوع المحلّ القريب الذي يقوم بنفسه لا بتقويم الشيء الحال فيه كاللون في الإنسان بل في الجسم، فإن ماهية الجسم لا تتقوم باللون بل اللون عارض يلحق بعد قوام ماهية الجسم بذاته، لا كصورة المائية في الماء، فإنها إذا فارقت عند انقلاب الماء هواء، كان المفارق ما تتبدل الماهية بسببه لا كالحرارة والبرودة إذا فارقت الماء، فإن الماهية لا تتبدّل فإنا إذا سُئلنا عن الحارّ والبارد ما هو ؟ قلنا هو ماء. وإذا سُئلنا : عن الهواء لم نقل إنه ماء. وإن أوردنا ثم وقلنا : ماء حارّ أو بارد ولم نُورد ههنا فنقول ماء قد تخلخل وانتشر فإن صورة المائية قد زالت .
والمتكلمون أيضاً يُسمون هذا أيضاً عَرَضاً، فإنهم يعنون بالعَرَض ما هو في محل، وهذه الصورة في محلّ والاصطلاح لا ينبغي أن ينازع فيه، فلكل فريق أن يصطلح في تخصيص العَرَض بما يريد ولكن لا يمكن إنكار الفرق بين الحرارة بالنسبة إلى الماء التي تزول عند البرودة وبين صورة المائية التي تزول عند انقلابه هواء، فإن الزائل ههنا يبدل المذكور في جواب ما هو والزائل ثم لا يبدله. والجوهر على اصطلاح المتكلمين عبارة عمّا ليس في محل، فصورة المائية ليس جوهراً، وعلى اصطلاح الفلاسفة عبارة عمّا ليس في موضوع، فالصورة عندهم جوهر والمعنى المشترك بين الماء والهواء إذا استحال الماء هواء يسمى عندهم أيضاً جوهراً وهو الهيولى.
Statement on substance. Know that existence is divided in a type of division into substance and accident. The name of each of substance and accident is ambiguous as previously. But we now intend from their totality one thing. So we mean by substance: that which exists not in a subject. And we mean by subject: the proximate locus which subsists by itself, not by the constitution of the thing that inheres in it, like color in a human but rather in a body. For the essence of a body is not constituted by color; rather color is an accident that attaches after the subsistence of the essence of the body by itself. Not like the form of waterness in water, for when it departs upon the transformation of water into air, the departing thing is that by which the essence changes. Not like heat and coldness when they depart from water, for the essence does not change. For if we are asked about the hot and cold 'what is it?' we say: it is water. And if we are asked about the air, we do not say it is water. And if we mention there and say 'hot water' or 'cold water', but not here we say 'water that has rarefied and expanded' for the form of waterness has ceased. And the mutakallimun also call this an accident, for they mean by accident that which is in a locus, and this form is in a locus. And terminology should not be disputed. For each group may stipulate in specifying accident as they wish, but it is impossible to deny the difference between heat with respect to water which disappears upon coldness, and the form of waterness which disappears upon its transformation into air. For what disappears here changes what is mentioned in the answer to 'what is it?', while what disappears there does not change it. And substance according to the terminology of the mutakallimun is an expression for what is not in a locus, so the form of waterness is not a substance. And according to the terminology of the philosophers, it is an expression for what is not in a subject, so form is according to them a substance, and the common meaning between water and air when water changes into air is also called by them a substance, and it is the hyh (prime matter).
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage defines substance and accident. The author begins by stating that existence is divided into substance and accident. The terms are ambiguous (mushtarak) as previously mentioned. They specify: substance is that which exists not in a subject (موضوع), and subject is the proximate locus that subsists by itself, not by the constitution of the thing that inheres in it. For example, color inheres in a body, but the body's essence is not constituted by color; color is an accident. In contrast, the form of waterness (صورة المائية) in water is such that when water changes into air, the essence changes—the answer to 'what is it?' changes from 'water' to 'air'. But when heat or coldness leaves water, the essence remains water. So heat is an accident, while waterness is not an accident in the same sense because it constitutes the essence. The author then notes that the mutakallimun (theologians) also call waterness an accident because they define accident as that which is in a locus (محل), and waterness is in a locus (the water). But he says terminological disputes are pointless; each group can define as they wish. The key point is the real distinction: one type of change alters the essence, the other does not. Finally, the author distinguishes between the mutakallimun's definition of substance (not in a locus) and the philosophers' definition (not in a subject). For the philosophers, the form (صورة) is a substance, and the underlying common matter (هيولى) is also a substance.

Key Terms
الجوهر
العرض
الموضوع
المحل
الصورة
الهيولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The classification of substance versus accident depends on whether the entity changes the essence; philosophers and mutakallimun differ on what counts as a 'subject'.

Particular vs Universal: The Key to Predication
Author distinguishes four types and predication conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given term as a particular or universal substance/accident and determine whether it can serve as a logical predicate.

Core Idea
Only universals can be predicates; particulars are always subjects.

In the previous lesson we understood the meaning of subject (mawdu'). Now we need to distinguish between the types of things that can be predicates. The key insight is that only universals — whether substance or accident — can be predicated. Particulars like Zayd or his specific whiteness cannot be predicates. This lesson will teach you to classify any term into one of four categories based on whether it exists in a subject and whether it is universal or particular.

Guided Path
1
Determine whether the term is in a subject
A substance is not in a subject; an accident is in a subject. For example, Zayd (a person) is not in something else, while whiteness is in a body. Decide first which category the term belongs to.
In the Aristotelian framework, 'in a subject' means existing in something else as a dependent entity.
2
Determine whether the term is universal or particular
A universal term applies to many (e.g., 'human' applies to Zayd, Amr, etc.). A particular term applies to only one individual (e.g., 'this human' or 'Zayd'). Use the test: can you say 'it is one of many'? If yes, it is universal.
Particulars are also called 'individuals'.
3
Combine the two determinations
Cross the two attributes to get four types: particular substance (not in subject, not universal), universal substance (not in subject, universal), particular accident (in subject, not universal), universal accident (in subject, universal).
4
Apply the predication rule
Only universals (whether substance or accident) can be predicates. For example, you can say 'Zayd is human' (human is universal) but not 'Zayd is Zayd' as a predication (Zayd is particular). Similarly, you can say 'whiteness is a color' (color is universal accident) but not 'this whiteness is whiteness' as a predication of the particular whiteness.
The rule: 'A thing can only be a predicate by virtue of being universal, whether accident or substance.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا فهم معنى الموضوع، فالفرق بينه وبين المحمول أن الجوهر ينقسم إلى ما ليس في الموضوع، ولا يمكن أن يكون محمولاً، وإلى ما ليس في موضوع، ويمكن حمله على موضوع والأول هو الجوهر الشخصي كزيد وعمرو. والثاني هو الجواهر الكلية كالإنسان والجسم والحيوان فإنا نشير إلى موضوع مثل زيد ونحمل هذه الجواهر عليه، وتقول زيد إنسان وحيوان وجسم، فيكون المحمول جوهراً لا عَرَضاً إلَّا أنه محمول عرف ذات الموضوع، وليس خارجاً ذاته، لا كالعَرَض إذا حمل على الجوهر فإنه يُعرف به شيء خارج عن ذات الموضوع، إذ البياض يحمل على الجوهر وهو خارج عن ذات الجوهر، ولذلك لا يحدّ هذا الموضوع بحدّ المحمول، إذ نقول في حدّ البياض: إنه لون يفرق البصر ولا يحدّ به الموضوع. وأما الإنسان والحيوان والجسم ونظائرها فنحملها على شخص زيد، ويحدّ هذه الجواهر بحدّ، وهو بعينه حدّ الموضوع، إذ نقول لزيد : إنه حيوان ناطق مائت، أو هو جسم ذو نفس حسّاس مُتحرّك بالإرادة؛ فبهذا يتهيأ الفرق بين الجواهر الكلية والجواهر الجزئية .
وأما الأعراض فجملتها في موضوع ولكنها تنقسم إلى ما يقال على موضوع بطريق الحمل عليه وإلى ما لا يحمل على موضوع فالمحمول على موضوع هي الأعراض الكلية كاللون مثلاً، فإنه يحمل على البياض والسواد وغيره، فيقال: البياض لون والسواد لون وأما الأعراض الشخصية فلا يمكن حملها ككتابة زيد وبياض شخص، إذ لا يمكن أن يحمل على شيء حتى يقال هو كتابة زيد أو بياض شخص، وإذا قلت زيد كاتب أو أبيض لم يكن ذلك حملاً للبياض بل معناه كتابة. ومهما قلنا هو ذو إنسان لم يكن الإنسان محمولاً، وكذا إذا قلنا: ذو بياض، فإذا الشيء إنما يمكن أن يكون محمولاً باعتبار كونه كلياً، عَرَضاً كان أو جوهراً. ومهما كان شخصاً لم يكن محمولاً، عَرَضاً كان أو جوهراً. وسيأتي حقيقة معنى الكلي في أحكام الوجود. فإن قيل: فالجوهر الكلّي أولى بمعنى الجوهرية أم الشخصي؟ قلنا : الجوهر الكلّي على ما سيأتي قوامه بالشخصيات، إذ لولاها لم تكن الكليات موجودة، فالشخص في الرتبة مُتقدم عليه، لكن الشخص
When the meaning of subject is understood, the difference between it and the predicate is that substance is divided into what is not in a subject and cannot be a predicate, and into what is not in a subject and can be predicated of a subject. The first is the particular substance like Zayd and Amr. The second is the universal substances like human, body, and animal. For we point to a subject like Zayd and predicate these substances of him, and we say 'Zayd is a human, an animal, a body'. So the predicate is a substance, not an accident. Except that it is a predicate that defines the essence of the subject, and is not external to its essence. Not like the accident when predicated of substance, for by it something external to the essence of the subject is known. Since whiteness is predicated of substance and it is external to the essence of substance. Therefore this subject is not defined by the definition of the predicate, for we say in the definition of whiteness: it is a color that disperses sight, and the subject is not defined by it. As for human, animal, body and their likes, we predicate them on the person Zayd, and these substances are defined by a definition, and it is the very definition of the subject. For we say of Zayd: he is a rational, mortal animal, or he is a body possessing a sensitive soul that moves by will. Thus the difference between universal substances and particular substances is prepared. As for accidents, all of them are in a subject, but they are divided into what is said of a subject by way of predication and into what is not predicated of a subject. So what is predicated of a subject are the universal accidents like color, for it is predicated of whiteness, blackness, etc., and it is said: whiteness is a color, blackness is a color. As for particular accidents, they cannot be predicated, like the writing of Zayd and the whiteness of a person, for it cannot be predicated of anything such that it is said 'it is the writing of Zayd' or 'the whiteness of a person'. And if you say 'Zayd is a writer' or 'white', that is not a predication of whiteness but its meaning is writing. And whenever we say 'he is the possessor of a human', the human is not a predicate, similarly when we say 'possessor of whiteness'. So a thing can only be a predicate by virtue of being universal, whether accident or substance. And whenever it is particular, it is not a predicate, whether accident or substance. And the reality of the meaning of universal will come in the rulings of existence. If it is asked: is the universal substance more entitled to the meaning of substancehood or the particular? We say: the universal substance, as will come, its subsistence is through particulars, for if they were not, the universals would not exist. So the particular is prior in rank to it, but the particular...
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage discusses the distinction between subject and predicate, and between particular and universal substances and accidents. After understanding the subject (locus), the author explains that substance is divided into two types: (1) particular substance (جوهر شخصي) which is not in a subject and cannot be predicated of anything (e.g., Zayd, Amr), and (2) universal substance (جوهر كلي) which is not in a subject but can be predicated of a subject (e.g., human, body, animal). When we predicate a universal substance like 'human' of Zayd, the predicate (human) is a substance, not an accident, because it defines the essence of the subject. In contrast, if we predicate an accident like 'white' of Zayd, the predicate is external to the essence. This is also shown by definition: the definition of a universal substance (e.g., human: rational mortal animal) is also the definition of the particular subject (Zayd). But the definition of an accident (e.g., whiteness) is not the definition of the subject. Then the author discusses accidents: all accidents exist in a subject, but some are universal and can be predicated (e.g., color predicated of whiteness), while particular accidents cannot be predicated (e.g., the whiteness of a specific person). The key is that only universals can be predicates, whether substance or accident. Particulars cannot be predicates. Finally, a question is raised: which is more entitled to the meaning of substance, the universal or the particular? The answer is that the universal subsists through the particulars, so the particular is prior in rank.

Key Terms
المحمول
الموضوع
الجوهر الشخصي
الجوهر الكلي
العرض الكلي
العرض الشخصي

B) Human

D) The writing of Zayd

Key Takeaway
Only universals — whether substance or accident — can serve as logical predicates; particulars are always subjects.

Priority of Individual Substance over Universal Substance
Priority of individual vs universal substance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why individual substance is prior in existence but universal substance is prior in intelligibility, using Al-Ghazali's reasoning.

Core Idea
Individuals exist; universals depend on them but make individuals intelligible.

Al-Ghazali poses a question: Is universal substance more deserving of the name 'substance' than the individual? His answer reveals a fundamental insight about the relationship between existence and knowledge. Let's walk through his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of substance
Universal substance (jawhar kulli) is predicated of many, like 'human'; individual substance (jawhar shakhsi) is a particular like Zayd.
2
Determine the basis of priority
Al-Ghazali asks which is more deserving of the meaning of substantiality. He answers by considering dependency.
3
Analyze the existential dependency
Universals subsist through individuals; without individuals, universals would not exist. Therefore, the individual is prior in existence (fi al-wujud).
This is an ontological priority.
4
Contrast intelligibility
The individual becomes intelligible only through the universal. So the universal is prior in intelligibility (fi al-ta'aqqul).
This is an epistemological priority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: فالجوهر الكلّي أولى بمعنى الجوهرية أم الشخصي؟ قلنا : الجوهر الكلّي على ما سيأتي قوامه بالشخصيات، إذ لولاها لم تكن الكليات موجودة، فالشخص في الرتبة مُتقدم عليه، لكن الشخص في صيرورته معقولاً يفتقر إلى الكلّي ولا يفتقر في الوجود إليه، وتحقيق هذا عند بيان معنى الكلي. فإن قيل : فما أقسام الجوهر؟ قلنا : إذا أريد بهذا الجوهر القائم لا في محلّ فقط أو القائم لا في موضوع انقسم إلى جسم، أعني إلى متحيز وغير متحيّز. والجسم ينقسم إلى مغتذ وغير مغتذ. والمغتذي ينقسم إلى حيوان وإلى غير حيوان والحيوان ينقسم إلى ناطق وغير ناطق وهذا تدخل فيه الحيوانات كلّها على اختلاف أصنافها وينفصل كلّ نوع بفصل يخصّه وإن كنا لا نشعر به . وغير المغتذي يدخل فيه السماء والكواكب والعناصر الأربعة والمعادن كلّها؛ فهذه أقسام الجواهر . وذهب أكثر المتكلمين أن الجواهر المتحيّزة كلّها جنس واحد، وإنما تختلف بأعراضها، إذ للجسم ماهية واحدة وهو كونه متحيّزاً مؤتلفاً فكونه حيا معناه قيام العلم والحياة .به والفلاسفة يقولون : إن هذه الجواهر مختلفة في أنفسها باختلاف حدودها، وإن الصفات المقومات لها هيئات للأشياء التي بتبدل ماهيتها يتبدّل جواب ما هو، ويوجب اختلافاً في تحقيق الذات وتحقيق الحق في هذين المذهبين ليس من غرضنا بل الغرض بيان معنى الجوهر وأقسامه. وقد حان القول في الكمية والمقدار
If it is said: Is the universal substance more deserving of the meaning of substantiality, or the individual? We say: The universal substance, as will come, subsists through individuals, for were it not for them, universals would not exist. So the individual is prior in rank to it, but the individual in becoming intelligible requires the universal and does not require it in existence. The verification of this is at the explanation of the meaning of universal. If it is said: What are the divisions of substance? We say: If what is meant by this substance is that which subsists not in a place only or subsists not in a subject, it divides into body, i.e., into extended and non-extended. Body divides into nourished and non-nourished. Nourished divides into animal and non-animal. Animal divides into rational and non-rational. This includes all animals of various kinds, and every species is distinguished by a specific differentia even if we are not aware of it. Non-nourished includes the sky, the planets, the four elements, and all minerals. These are the divisions of substances. Most theologians hold that all extended substances are one genus and differ only by their accidents, for body has one essence: being an extended composite. So its being alive means that knowledge and life subsist in it. The philosophers say that these substances differ in themselves by their definitions, and that the constitutive attributes are forms of things such that when their essence changes, the answer to 'what is it?' changes, causing a difference in the realization of essence. The verification of truth in these two doctrines is not our aim; rather our aim is to explain the meaning of substance and its divisions. Now it is time to speak about quantity and magnitude.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage addresses two issues: first, a question about priority between universal and individual substance. Al-Ghazali answers that the individual is prior in existence (since universals depend on individuals for their existence), but the universal is prior in intelligibility (since we cannot understand the individual without the universal). This is a classic epistemological point. Second, he provides a classification of substance (jawhar) when taken as 'not in a place' (theologians' view) or 'not in a subject' (philosophers' view). Substance is divided into body (mutaḥayyiz, extended) and non-body (ghayr mutaḥayyiz). Body further divides into nourished (e.g., animals, plants) and non-nourished (e.g., minerals, celestial bodies). Nourished divides into animal and non-animal (plants). Animal divides into rational (humans) and non-rational (other animals). Each species has its own differentia. Non-nourished includes the sky, planets, four elements, and minerals. Al-Ghazali then notes the disagreement between theologians and philosophers: theologians say all extended substances are one genus differing only by accidents, while philosophers say they differ in essence. He does not adjudicate but simply states both views as part of explaining substance.

Key Terms
جوهر كلي
جوهر شخصي

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) In existence
B) In intelligibility
C) In definition
D) In predication

Key Takeaway
Individual substances are prior in existence, but universals are prior in intelligibility; both are needed for a complete understanding of substance.

Classification of Substance into Body and Non-Body
Classification of substance into bodies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given existing thing into Al-Ghazali's substance taxonomy, explaining the logical divisions.

Core Idea
Substance is either extended (body) or not; everything else is a property of bodies.

After clarifying the priority of individuals, Al-Ghazali provides a taxonomy of substance. This classification is essential for understanding the categories of being in Islamic metaphysics. Let's work through his division step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary division
Substance (understood as 'not in a place' or 'not in a subject') divides into body (mutaḥayyiz, extended) and non-body (ghayr mutaḥayyiz).
Body occupies space.
2
Subdivide body into nourished and non-nourished
Body divides into that which can be nourished (al-mughtadhi) and that which cannot (ghayr al-mughtadhi). Nourished bodies include plants and animals; non-nourished include minerals, celestial bodies.
3
Subdivide nourished into animal and non-animal
Nourished divides into animal (hayawan) and non-animal (e.g., plants). Animals are capable of sensation and movement.
4
Subdivide animal into rational and non-rational
Animal divides into rational (natiq) and non-rational (ghayr natiq). Rational animals are humans; non-rational include all other animals.
Each species has a specific differentia, even if we are not aware of it.
5
Identify the non-nourished category
Non-nourished includes the sky, planets, four elements, and minerals. These are not capable of nourishment.
Al-Ghazali notes a disagreement: theologians say all bodies are one genus differing by accidents; philosophers say they differ in essence.

Source Text
